    <div class="sidebar-with-breadcrumbs-layout">
        <div class="sidebar-with-breadcrumbs-layout__breadcrumbs">

                <span aria-current="page">RAMOS v. LOUISIANA</span>
            </li>
        
    </ol>
</nav>

        </div>
        <div class="sidebar-with-breadcrumbs-layout__content">
            <div class="caselawTitle section">
    <h1 class="caselaw-title">RAMOS<em> v. LOUISIANA</em> (2020)</h1>

</div>
<div class="caselawHeading section">
    <div class="caselaw-content searchable-content">
        <h2 class="caselaw-content__title">United States Supreme Court</h2>
        <h3 class="caselaw-content__title">RAMOS<em> v. LOUISIANA</em>(2020)</h3>
        <h3 class="caselaw-content__title">No. 18-5924</h3>
        <h3 class="caselaw-content__title"><span class="tab">Argued: October 07, 2019</span>Decided: April 20, 2020</h3>
        <h3 class="caselaw-content__title"></h3>
        <h3 class="caselaw-content__title"></h3>
    </div>

</div>
<div class="customizedContent section">
</div>
<div class="caselawContent section">
    <div id="caselaw-content" class="caselaw-content searchable-content">
        <p>In 48 States and federal court, a single juror's vote to acquit is enough to prevent a conviction.  But two States, Louisiana and Oregon, have long punished people based on 10-to-2 verdicts.  In this case, petitioner Evangelisto Ramos was convicted of a serious crime in a Louisiana court by a 10-to-2 jury verdict.  Instead of the mistrial he would have received almost anywhere else, Ramos was sentenced to life without parole.  He contests his conviction by a nonunanimous jury as an unconstitutional denial of the Sixth Amendment right to a jury trial.  </p>
<p><em>Held</em>:&nbsp;The judgment is reversed.</p>
<p>2016-1199 (La. App. 4 Cir. 11/2/17), 231 So.&nbsp;3d 44, reversed.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Gorsuch</small></span> delivered the opinion of the Court with respect to Parts I, II-A, III, and IV-B-1, concluding that the Sixth Amendment right to a jury trial--as incorporated against the States by way of the Fourteenth Amendment--requires a unanimous verdict to convict a defendant of a serious offense.  Pp.&nbsp;3-9, 11-15, 20-23.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;(a)&nbsp;The Constitution's text and structure clearly indicate that the Sixth Amendment term "trial by an impartial jury" carries with it <em>some</em> meaning about the content and requirements of a jury trial.  One such requirement is that a jury must reach a unanimous verdict in order to convict.  Juror unanimity emerged as a vital common law right in 14th-century England, appeared in the early American state constitutions, and provided the backdrop against which the Sixth Amendment was drafted and ratified.  Postadoption treatises and 19th-century American legal treatises confirm this understanding.  This Court has commented on the Sixth Amendment's unanimity requirement no fewer than 13 times over more than 120 years, see, <em>e.g., Thompson</em> v. <em>Utah,</em> 170 U.&nbsp;S. 343, 351; <em>Patton</em> v. <em>United States,</em> 281 U.&nbsp;S. 276, 288, and has also explained that the Sixth Amendment right to a jury trial is incorporated against the States under the Fourteenth Amendment, <em>Duncan</em> v. <em>Louisiana,</em> 391 U.&nbsp;S. 145, 148-150.  Thus, if the jury trial right requires a unanimous verdict in federal court, it requires no less in state court.  Pp.&nbsp;3-7.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;(b)&nbsp;Louisiana's and Oregon's unconventional schemes were first confronted in <em>Apodaca</em> v. <em>Oregon,</em> 406 U.&nbsp;S. 404, and <em>Johnson</em> v. <em>Louisiana,</em> 406 U.&nbsp;S. 356, in a badly fractured set of opinions.  A four-Justice plurality, questioning whether unanimity serves an important "function" in "contemporary society," concluded that unanimity's costs outweighed its benefits.  <em>Apodaca</em>, 406 U.&nbsp;S., at 410.  Four dissenting Justices recognized that the Sixth Amendment requires unanimity, and that the guarantee is fully applicable against the States under the Fourteenth Amendment.  The remaining Justice, Justice Powell, adopted a "dual-track" incorporation approach.  He agreed that the Sixth Amendment requires unanimity but believed that the Fourteenth Amendment does not render this guarantee fully applicable against the States--even though the dual-track incorporation approach had been rejected by the Court nearly a decade earlier, see <em>Malloy</em> v. <em>Hogan,</em> 378 U.&nbsp;S. 1, 10-11.  Pp.&nbsp;7-9.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;(c)&nbsp;The best Louisiana can suggest is that all of the Court's prior statements that the Sixth Amendment <em>does</em> require unanimity are dicta.  But the State offers no hint as to why the Court would walk away from those statements now and does not dispute the fact that the common law required unanimity.  Instead, it argues that the Sixth Amendment's drafting history--in particular, that the original House version's explicit unanimity references were removed in the Senate version--reveals the framer's intent to leave this particular feature of the common law behind.  But that piece of drafting history could just as easily support the inference that the language was removed as surplusage because the right was so plainly understood to be included in the right to trial by jury.  Finally, the State invites the Court to perform a cost-benefit analysis on the historic features of common law jury trials and to conclude that unanimity does not make the cut.  The dangers of that approach, however, can be seen in <em>Apodaca</em>, where the plurality subjected the ancient guarantee of a unanimous jury verdict to its own functionalist assessment.  Pp.&nbsp;11-15.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;(d)&nbsp;Factors traditionally considered by the Court when determining whether to preserve precedent on <em>stare decisis </em>grounds do not favor upholding <em>Apodaca</em>.  See <em>Franchise Tax Bd. of Cal.</em> v. <em>Hyatt</em>, 587 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___.  Starting with the quality of <em>Apodaca</em>'s reasoning, the plurality opinion and separate concurring opinion were gravely mistaken.  And <em>Apodaca</em> sits uneasily with 120 years of preceding case law.  When  it comes to reliance interests, neither Louisiana nor Oregon claims anything like the prospective economic, regulatory, or social disruption litigants seeking to preserve precedent usually invoke.  The fact that Louisiana and Oregon may need to retry defendants convicted of felonies by nonunanimous verdicts whose cases are still pending on direct appeal will surely impose a cost, but new rules of criminal procedure usually do, see<em>, e.g., United States</em> v. <em>Booker</em>, 543 U.&nbsp;S. 220, and prior convictions in only two States are potentially affected here.  Pp.&nbsp;20-23.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Gorsuch</small></span>, joined by <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Ginsburg</small></span> and <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Breyer,</small></span> concluded in Part IV-A that <em>Apodaca</em> lacks precedential force.  Treating that case as precedential would require embracing the dubious proposition that a single Justice writing only for himself has the authority to bind this Court to already rejected propositions.  No prior case has made such a suggestion.  Pp.&nbsp;16-20.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Gorsuch</small></span>, joined by <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Ginsburg</small></span>, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Breyer</small></span>, and <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor</small></span>, concluded in Parts IV-B-2 and V that Louisiana's and Oregon's reliance interests in the security of their final criminal judgments do not favor upholding <em>Apodaca</em>.  Worries that defendants whose appeals are already complete might seek to challenge their nonunanimous convictions through collateral review are overstated.  Cf. <em>Teague</em> v. <em>Lane</em>, 489 U.&nbsp;S. 288.  <em>Apodaca</em>'s reliance interests are not boosted by Louisiana's recent decision to bar the use of nonunanimous jury verdicts.  A ruling for Louisiana would invite other States to relax their own unanimity requirements, and Louisiana continues to allow nonunanimous verdicts for crimes committed before 2019.  Pp.&nbsp;23-26.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas </small></span>concluded that Ramos' felony conviction by a nonunanimous jury is unconstitutional because the Sixth Amendment's protection against nonunanimous felony guilty verdicts applies against the States through the Privileges or Immunities Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment, not the Due Process Clause.  Pp.&nbsp;1-9.</p>
<p><span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Gorsuch, J., </small></span>announced the judgment of the Court, and delivered the opinion of the Court with respect to Parts I, II-A, III, and IV-B-1, in which <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Ginsburg</small></span>, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Breyer</small></span>, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor</small></span>, and <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kavanaugh</small></span>, JJ., joined, an opinion with respect to Parts II-B, IV-B-2, and V, in which <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Ginsburg</small></span>, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Breyer</small></span>, and <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor</small></span>, JJ., joined, and an opinion with respect to Part IV-A, in which <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Ginsburg</small></span> and <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Breyer</small></span>, JJ., joined.  <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor</small></span>, J., filed an opinion concurring as to all but Part IV-A.  <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kavanaugh</small></span>, J., filed an opinion concurring in part.  <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Thomas</small></span>, J., filed an opinion concurring in the judgment.  <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Alito</small></span>, J., filed a dissenting opinion, in which <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Roberts</small></span>, C.&nbsp;J., joined, and in which <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kagan</small></span>, J., joined as to all but Part III-D.</p>
<p></p><hr>




<p align="CENTER">Opinion of the Court</p>
<p align="CENTER"> 590 U.&nbsp;S. ____ (2020)</p>

<p><font size="-1">NOTICE: This opinion is subject to formal revision before publication in the preliminary print of the United States Reports. Readers are requested to notify the Reporter of Decisions, Supreme Court of the United States, Washington, D.&nbsp;C. 20543, of any typographical or other formal errors, in order that corrections may be made before the preliminary print goes to press.</font></p>

<p align="CENTER">No. 18-5924</p>


<center><h2><p align="CENTER">EVANGELISTO RAMOS, PETITIONER <em>v.</em> LOUISIANA</p>
</h2></center><center><h4><p align="CENTER"><span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>on writ of certiorari to the court of appeal</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>of louisiana, fourth circuit</small></span></p>
</h4></center><center><p align="CENTER">[April 20, 2020]</p>
</center><p></p><hr>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Gorsuch</small></span> announced the judgment of the Court and delivered the opinion of the Court with respect to Parts I, II-A, III, and IV-B-1, an opinion with respect to Parts II-B, IV-B-2, and V, in which <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Ginsburg</small></span>, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Breyer</small></span>, and <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Sotomayor</small></span> join, and an opinion with respect to Part IV-A, in which <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Ginsburg</small></span> and <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Breyer</small></span> join.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Accused of a serious crime, Evangelisto Ramos insisted on his innocence and invoked his right to a jury trial.  Eventually, 10 jurors found the evidence against him persuasive.  But a pair of jurors believed that the State of Louisiana had failed to prove Mr. Ramos's guilt beyond reasonable doubt; they voted to acquit.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In 48 States and federal court, a single juror's vote to acquit is enough to prevent a conviction.  But not in Louisiana.  Along with Oregon, Louisiana has long punished people based on 10-to-2 verdicts like the one here.  So instead of the mistrial he would have received almost anywhere else, Mr. Ramos was sentenced to life in prison without the possibility of parole.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Why do Louisiana and Oregon allow nonunanimous convictions?  Though it's hard to say why these laws persist, their origins are clear.  Louisiana first endorsed nonunanimous verdicts for serious crimes at a constitutional convention in 1898.  According to one committee chairman, the avowed purpose of that convention was to "establish the supremacy of the white race," and the resulting document included many of the trappings of the Jim Crow era: a poll tax, a combined literacy and property ownership test, and a grandfather clause that in practice exempted white residents from the most onerous of these requirements.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 1" name="FRopinion1.1" href="#FNopinion1.1">1</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Nor was it only the prospect of African-Americans voting that concerned the delegates.  Just a week before the convention, the U. S. Senate passed a resolution calling for an investigation into whether Louisiana was systemically excluding African-Americans from juries.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 2" name="FRopinion1.2" href="#FNopinion1.2">2</a></sup>  Seeking to avoid unwanted national attention, and aware that this Court would strike down any policy of overt discrimination against African-American jurors as a violation of the Fourteenth Amendment,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 3" name="FRopinion1.3" href="#FNopinion1.3">3</a></sup> the delegates sought to undermine  African-American participation on juries in another way.  With a careful eye on racial demographics, the convention delegates sculpted a "facially race-neutral" rule permitting 10-to-2 verdicts in order "to ensure that African-American juror service would be meaningless."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 4" name="FRopinion1.4" href="#FNopinion1.4">4</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Adopted in the 1930s, Oregon's rule permitting nonunanimous verdicts can be similarly traced to the rise of the Ku Klux Klan and efforts to dilute "the influence of racial, ethnic, and religious minorities on Oregon juries."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 5" name="FRopinion1.5" href="#FNopinion1.5">5</a></sup>  In fact, no one before us contests any of this; courts in both Louisiana and Oregon have frankly acknowledged that race was a motivating factor in the adoption of their States' respective nonunanimity rules.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 6" name="FRopinion1.6" href="#FNopinion1.6">6</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;We took this case to decide whether the Sixth Amendment right to a jury trial--as incorporated against the States by way of the Fourteenth Amendment--requires a unanimous verdict to convict a defendant of a serious offense.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 7" name="FRopinion1.7" href="#FNopinion1.7">7</a></sup>  Louisiana insists that this Court has never definitively passed on the question and urges us to find its practice consistent with the Sixth Amendment.  By contrast, the dissent doesn't try to defend Louisiana's law on Sixth or Fourteenth Amendment grounds; tacitly, it seems to admit that the Constitution forbids States from using nonunanimous juries.  Yet, unprompted by Louisiana, the dissent suggests our precedent requires us to rule for the State anyway.  What explains all this?  To answer the puzzle, it's necessary to say a bit more about the merits of the question presented, the relevant precedent, and, at last, the consequences that follow from saying what we know to be true.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">I</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Sixth Amendment promises that "[i]n all criminal prosecutions, the accused shall enjoy the right to a speedy and public trial, by an impartial jury of the State and district wherein the crime shall have been committed, which district shall have been previously ascertained by law."  The Amendment goes on to preserve other rights for criminal defendants but says nothing else about what a "trial by an impartial jury" entails.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Still, the promise of a jury trial surely meant <em>something</em>--otherwise, there would have been no reason to write it down.  Nor would it have made any sense to spell out the places from which jurors should be drawn if their powers as jurors could be freely abridged by statute.  Imagine a constitution that allowed a "jury trial" to mean nothing but a single person rubberstamping convictions without hearing any evidence--but simultaneously insisting that the lone juror come from a specific judicial district "previously ascertained by law."  And if that's not enough, imagine a constitution that included the same hollow guarantee <em>twice</em>--not only in the Sixth Amendment, but also in Article III.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 8" name="FRopinion1.8" href="#FNopinion1.8">8</a></sup>  No:  The text and structure of the Constitution clearly suggest that the term "trial by an impartial jury" carried with it <em>some</em> meaning about the content and requirements of a jury trial.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;One of these requirements was unanimity.  Wherever we might look to determine what the term "trial by an impartial jury trial" meant at the time of the Sixth Amendment's adoption--whether it's the common law, state practices in the founding era, or opinions and treatises written soon afterward--the answer is unmistakable.  A jury must reach a unanimous verdict in order to convict.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The requirement of juror unanimity emerged in 14thcentury England and was soon accepted as a vital right protected by the common law.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 9" name="FRopinion1.9" href="#FNopinion1.9">9</a></sup>  As Blackstone explained, no person could be found guilty of a serious crime unless "the truth of every accusation .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. should .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. be confirmed by the unanimous suffrage of twelve of his equals and neighbors, indifferently chosen, and superior to all suspicion."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 10" name="FRopinion1.10" href="#FNopinion1.10">10</a></sup>  A "&nbsp;'verdict, taken from eleven, was no verdict'&nbsp;" at all.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 11" name="FRopinion1.11" href="#FNopinion1.11">11</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;This same rule applied in the young American States.  Six State Constitutions explicitly required unanimity.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 12" name="FRopinion1.12" href="#FNopinion1.12">12</a></sup>  Another four preserved the right to a jury trial in more general terms.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 13" name="FRopinion1.13" href="#FNopinion1.13">13</a></sup>  But the variations did not matter much; consistent with the common law, state courts appeared to regard unanimity as an essential feature of the jury trial.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 14" name="FRopinion1.14" href="#FNopinion1.14">14</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;It was against this backdrop that James Madison drafted and the States ratified the Sixth Amendment in 1791.  By that time, unanimous verdicts had been required for about 400 years.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 15" name="FRopinion1.15" href="#FNopinion1.15">15</a></sup>  If the term "trial by an impartial jury" carried any meaning at all, it surely included a requirement as long and widely accepted as unanimity.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Influential, postadoption treatises confirm this understanding.  For example, in 1824, Nathan Dane<em> </em>reported as fact that the U.&nbsp;S. Constitution required unanimity in criminal jury trials for serious offenses.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 16" name="FRopinion1.16" href="#FNopinion1.16">16</a></sup>  A few years later, Justice Story explained in his Commentaries on the Constitution<em> </em>that "in common cases, the law not only presumes every man innocent, until he is proved guilty; but unanimity in the verdict of the jury is indispensable."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 17" name="FRopinion1.17" href="#FNopinion1.17">17</a></sup>  Similar statements can be found in American legal treatises throughout the 19th century.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 18" name="FRopinion1.18" href="#FNopinion1.18">18</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Nor is this a case where the original public meaning was lost to time and only recently recovered.  This Court has, repeatedly and over many years, recognized that the Sixth Amendment requires unanimity.  As early as 1898, the Court said that a defendant enjoys a "constitutional right to demand that his liberty should not be taken from him except by the joint action of the court and the unanimous verdict of a jury of twelve persons."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 19" name="FRopinion1.19" href="#FNopinion1.19">19</a></sup>  A few decades later, the Court elaborated that the Sixth Amendment affords a right to "a trial by jury as understood and applied at common law, .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. includ[ing] all the essential elements as they were recognized in this country and England when the Constitution was adopted."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 20" name="FRopinion1.20" href="#FNopinion1.20">20</a></sup>  And, the Court observed, this  includes a requirement "that the verdict should be unanimous."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 21" name="FRopinion1.21" href="#FNopinion1.21">21</a></sup>  In all, this Court has commented on the Sixth Amendment's unanimity requirement no fewer than 13<em> </em>times over more than 120 years.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 22" name="FRopinion1.22" href="#FNopinion1.22">22</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;There can be no question either that the Sixth Amendment's unanimity requirement applies to state and federal criminal trials equally.  This Court has long explained that the Sixth Amendment right to a jury trial is "fundamental to the American scheme of justice" and incorporated against the States under the Fourteenth Amendment.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 23" name="FRopinion1.23" href="#FNopinion1.23">23</a></sup>  This Court has long explained, too, that incorporated provisions of the Bill of Rights bear the same content when asserted against States as they do when asserted against the federal government.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 24" name="FRopinion1.24" href="#FNopinion1.24">24</a></sup>  So if the Sixth Amendment's right to a jury trial requires a unanimous verdict to support a conviction in federal court, it requires no less in state court.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">II</p>
</h2><h3><p align="CENTER">A</p>
</h3><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;How, despite these seemingly straightforward principles, have Louisiana's and Oregon's laws managed to hang on for so long?  It turns out that the Sixth Amendment's otherwise simple story took a strange turn in 1972.  That year, the Court confronted these States' unconventional schemes for the first time--in <em>Apodaca </em>v. <em>Oregon</em><sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 25" name="FRopinion1.25" href="#FNopinion1.25">25</a></sup> and a companion case, <em>Johnson </em>v.<em> Louisiana</em>.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 26" name="FRopinion1.26" href="#FNopinion1.26">26</a></sup>  Ultimately, the Court could do no more than issue a badly fractured set of opinions.  Four dissenting Justices would not have hesitated to strike down the States' laws, recognizing that the Sixth Amendment requires unanimity and that this guarantee is fully applicable against the States under the Fourteenth Amendment.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 27" name="FRopinion1.27" href="#FNopinion1.27">27</a></sup>  But a four-Justice plurality took a very different view of the Sixth Amendment.  These Justices declared that the real question before them was whether unanimity serves an important "function" in "contemporary society."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 28" name="FRopinion1.28" href="#FNopinion1.28">28</a></sup>  Then, having reframed the question, the plurality wasted few words before concluding that unanimity's costs outweigh its benefits in the modern era, so the Sixth Amendment should not stand in the way of Louisiana or Oregon.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The ninth Member of the Court adopted a position that was neither here nor there.  On the one hand, Justice Powell agreed that, as a matter of "history and precedent, .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. the Sixth Amendment requires a unanimous jury verdict to convict."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 29" name="FRopinion1.29" href="#FNopinion1.29">29</a></sup>  But, on the other hand, he argued that the Fourteenth Amendment does not render this guarantee against the federal government fully applicable against the States.  In this way, Justice Powell doubled down on his belief in "dual-track" incorporation--the idea that a single right can mean two different things depending on whether it is being invoked against the federal or a state government.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Justice Powell acknowledged that his argument for dual-track incorporation came "late in the day."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 30" name="FRopinion1.30" href="#FNopinion1.30">30</a></sup>  Late it was.  The Court had already, nearly a decade earlier, "rejected the notion that the Fourteenth Amendment applies to the States only a 'watered-down, subjective version of the individual guarantees of the Bill of Rights.'&nbsp;"<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 31" name="FRopinion1.31" href="#FNopinion1.31">31</a></sup>  It's a point we've restated many times since, too, including as recently as last year.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 32" name="FRopinion1.32" href="#FNopinion1.32">32</a></sup>  Still, Justice Powell frankly explained, he was "unwillin[g]" to follow the Court's precedents.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 33" name="FRopinion1.33" href="#FNopinion1.33">33</a></sup>  So he offered up the essential fifth vote to uphold Mr. Apodaca's conviction--if based only on a view of the Fourteenth Amendment that he knew was (and remains) foreclosed by precedent.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">B</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In the years following <em>Apodaca</em>, both Louisiana and Oregon chose to continue allowing nonunanimous verdicts.  But their practices have always stood on shaky ground.  After all, while Justice Powell's vote secured a favorable judgment for the States in <em>Apodaca</em>, it's never been clear what rationale could support a similar result in future cases.  Only two possibilities exist:  Either the Sixth Amendment allows nonunanimous verdicts, or the Sixth Amendment's guarantee of a jury trial applies with less force to the States under the Fourteenth Amendment.  Yet, as we've seen, both bear their problems.  In <em>Apodaca</em> itself, a majority of Justices--including Justice Powell--recognized that the Sixth Amendment demands unanimity, just as our cases have long said.  And this Court's precedents, both then and now, prevent the Court from applying the Sixth Amendment to the States in some mutated and diminished form under the Fourteenth Amendment.  So what could we possibly describe as the "holding" of <em>Apodaca</em>?</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Really, no one has found a way to make sense of it.  In later cases, this Court has labeled <em>Apodaca</em> an "exception," "unusual," and in any event "not an endorsement" of Justice Powell's view of incorporation.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 34" name="FRopinion1.34" href="#FNopinion1.34">34</a></sup>  At the same time, we have continued to recognize the historical need for unanimity.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 35" name="FRopinion1.35" href="#FNopinion1.35">35</a></sup>  We've been studiously ambiguous, even inconsistent, about what <em>Apodaca</em> might mean.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 36" name="FRopinion1.36" href="#FNopinion1.36">36</a></sup>  To its credit, Louisiana acknowledges the problem.  The State expressly tells us it is not "asking the Court to accord Justice Powell's solo opinion in <em>Apodaca</em> precedential force."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 37" name="FRopinion1.37" href="#FNopinion1.37">37</a></sup>  Instead, in an effort to win today's case, Louisiana embraces the idea that everything is up for grabs.  It contends that this Court has never definitively ruled on the propriety of nonunanimous juries under the Sixth Amendment--and that we should use this case to hold for the first time that nonunanimous juries are permissible in state and federal courts alike.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">III</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Louisiana's approach may not be quite as tough as trying to defend Justice Powell's dual-track theory of incorporation, but it's pretty close.  How does the State deal with the fact this Court has said 13 times over 120 years that the Sixth Amendment <em>does</em> require unanimity?  Or the fact that five Justices in <em>Apodaca</em> said the same?  The best the State can offer is to suggest that all these statements came in dicta.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 38" name="FRopinion1.38" href="#FNopinion1.38">38</a></sup>  But even supposing (without granting) that Louisiana is right and it's dicta all the way down, why would the Court now walk away from many of its own statements about the Constitution's meaning?  And what about the prior 400 years of English and American cases requiring unanimity--should we dismiss all those as dicta too?</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Sensibly, Louisiana doesn't dispute that the common law required unanimity.  Instead, it argues that the drafting history of the Sixth Amendment reveals an intent by the framers to leave this particular feature behind.  The State points to the fact that Madison's proposal for the Sixth Amendment originally read:  "The trial of all crimes .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. shall be by an impartial jury of freeholders of the vicinage, with the requisite of unanimity for conviction, of the right of challenge, and other accustomed requisites.&nbsp;.&nbsp;.&nbsp;."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 39" name="FRopinion1.39" href="#FNopinion1.39">39</a></sup>  Louisiana notes that the House of Representatives approved this text with minor modifications.  Yet, the State stresses, the Senate replaced "impartial jury of freeholders of the vicinage" with "impartial jury of the State and district wherein the crime shall have been committed" and also removed the explicit references to unanimity, the right of challenge, and "other accustomed requisites."  In light of these revisions, Louisiana would have us infer an intent to abandon the common law's traditional unanimity requirement.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;But this snippet of drafting history could just as easily support the opposite inference.  Maybe the Senate deleted the language about unanimity, the right of challenge, and "other accustomed prerequisites" because all this was so plainly included in the promise of a "trial by an impartial jury" that Senators considered the language surplusage.  The truth is that we have little contemporaneous evidence shedding light on why the Senate acted as it did.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 40" name="FRopinion1.40" href="#FNopinion1.40">40</a></sup>  So rather than dwelling on text left on the cutting room floor, we are much better served by interpreting the language Congress retained and the States ratified.  And, as we've seen, at the time of the Amendment's adoption, the right to a jury trial <em>meant</em> a trial in which the jury renders a unanimous verdict.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Further undermining Louisiana's inference about the drafting history is the fact it proves too much.  If the Senate's deletion of the word "unanimity" changed the meaning of the text that remains, then the same would seemingly have to follow for the other deleted words as well.  So it's not just unanimity that died in the Senate, but all the "other accustomed requisites" associated with the common law jury trial right--<em>i.e.,</em> <em>everything</em> history might have taught us about what it means to have a jury trial.  Taking the State's argument from drafting history to its logical conclusion would thus leave the right to a "trial by jury" devoid of meaning.  A right mentioned twice in the Constitution would be reduced to an empty promise.  That can't be right.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Faced with this hard fact, Louisiana's only remaining option is to invite us to distinguish between the historic features of common law jury trials that (we think) serve "important enough" functions to migrate silently into the Sixth Amendment and those that don't.  And, on the State's account, we should conclude that unanimity isn't worthy enough to make the trip.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;But to see the dangers of Louisiana's overwise<em> </em>approach, there's no need to look any further than <em>Apodaca</em> itself.  There, four Justices, pursuing the functionalist approach Louisiana espouses, began by describing the "&nbsp;'essential'&nbsp;" benefit of a jury trial as "&nbsp;'the interposition .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. of the commonsense judgment of a group of laymen'&nbsp;" between the defendant and the possibility of an "&nbsp;'overzealous prosecutor.'&nbsp;"<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 41" name="FRopinion1.41" href="#FNopinion1.41">41</a></sup>  And measured against that muddy yardstick, they quickly concluded that requiring 12 rather than 10 votes to convict offers no meaningful improvement.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 42" name="FRopinion1.42" href="#FNopinion1.42">42</a></sup>  Meanwhile, these Justices argued, States have good and important reasons for dispensing with unanimity, such as seeking to reduce the rate of hung juries.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 43" name="FRopinion1.43" href="#FNopinion1.43">43</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Who can profess confidence in a breezy cost-benefit analysis like that?  Lost in the accounting are the racially discriminatory <em>reasons</em> that Louisiana and Oregon adopted their peculiar rules in the first place.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 44" name="FRopinion1.44" href="#FNopinion1.44">44</a></sup>  What's more, the plurality never explained why the promised benefit of abandoning unanimity--reducing the rate of hung juries--always scores as a credit, not a cost.  But who can say whether any particular hung jury is a waste, rather than an example of a jury doing exactly what the plurality said it should--deliberating carefully and safeguarding against overzealous prosecutions?  And what about the fact, too, that some studies suggest that the elimination of unanimity has only a small effect on the rate of hung juries?<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 45" name="FRopinion1.45" href="#FNopinion1.45">45</a></sup>  Or the fact that others profess to have found that requiring unanimity may provide other possible benefits, including more open-minded and more thorough deliberations?<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 46" name="FRopinion1.46" href="#FNopinion1.46">46</a></sup>  It seems  the <em>Apodaca</em> plurality never even conceived of such  possibilities.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Our real objection here isn't that the <em>Apodaca</em> plurality's cost-benefit analysis was too skimpy.  The deeper problem is that the plurality subjected the ancient guarantee of a unanimous jury verdict to its own functionalist assessment in the first place.  And Louisiana asks us to repeat the error today, just replacing <em>Apodaca</em>'s functionalist assessment with our own updated version.  All this overlooks the fact that, at the time of the Sixth Amendment's adoption, the right to trial by jury <em>included </em>a right to a unanimous verdict.  When the American people chose to enshrine that right in the Constitution, they weren't suggesting fruitful topics for future cost-benefit analyses.  They were seeking to ensure that their children's children would enjoy the same hard-won liberty they enjoyed.  As judges, it is not our role to reassess whether the right to a unanimous jury is "important enough" to retain.  With humility, we must accept that this right may serve purposes evading our current notice.  We are entrusted to preserve and protect that liberty, not balance it away aided by no more than social  statistics.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 47" name="FRopinion1.47" href="#FNopinion1.47">47</a></sup></p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">IV</p>
</h2><h3><p align="CENTER">A</p>
</h3><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;If Louisiana's path to an affirmance is a difficult one, the dissent's is trickier still.  The dissent doesn't dispute that the Sixth Amendment protects the right to a unanimous jury verdict, or that the Fourteenth Amendment extends this right to state-court trials.  But, it insists, we must affirm Mr. Ramos's conviction anyway.  Why?  Because the doctrine of <em>stare decisis</em> supposedly commands it.  There are two independent reasons why that answer falls short.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In the first place and as we've seen, not even Louisiana tries to suggest that <em>Apodaca</em> supplies a governing precedent.  Remember, Justice Powell agreed that the Sixth Amendment requires a unanimous verdict to convict, so he would have no objection to that aspect of our holding today.  Justice Powell reached a different result only by relying on a dual-track theory of incorporation that a majority of the Court had already rejected (and continues to reject).  And to accept <em>that</em> reasoning as precedential, we would have to embrace a new and dubious proposition:  that a single Justice writing only for himself has the authority to bind this Court to propositions it has already rejected.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;This is not the rule, and for good reason--it would do more to destabilize than honor precedent.  To see how, consider a hypothetical.  Suppose we face a question of first impression under the Fourth Amendment:  whether a State must obtain a warrant before reading a citizen's email in the hands of an Internet provider and using that email as evidence in a criminal trial.  Imagine this question splits the Court, with four Justices finding the Fourth Amendment requires a warrant and four Justices finding no such requirement.  The ninth Justice agrees that the Fourth Amendment requires a warrant, but takes an idiosyncratic view of the consequences of violating that right.  In her view, the exclusionary rule has gone too far, and should only apply when the defendant is prosecuted for a felony.  Because the case before her happens to involve only a misdemeanor, she provides the ninth vote to affirm a conviction based on evidence secured by a warrantless search.  Of course, this Court has longstanding precedent requiring the suppression of all evidence obtained in unconstitutional searches and seizures.  <em>Mapp</em> v. <em>Ohio</em>, 367 U.&nbsp;S. 643 (1961).  But like Justice Powell, our hypothetical ninth Justice sticks to her view and expressly rejects this Court's precedent.  Like Justice Powell, this Justice's vote would be essential to the judgment.  So if, as the dissent suggests, <em>that </em>is enough to displace precedent, would <em>Mapp</em>'s exclusionary rule<em> </em>now be limited to felony prosecutions?</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp; Admittedly, this example comes from our imagination.  It has to, because no case has before suggested that a single Justice may overrule precedent.  But if the Court were to embrace the dissent's view of <em>stare decisis</em>, it would not stay imaginary for long.  Every occasion on which the Court is evenly split would present an opportunity for single Justices to overturn precedent to bind future majorities.  Rather than advancing the goals of predictability and reliance lying behind the doctrine of <em>stare decisis</em>, such an approach would impair them.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The dissent contends that, in saying this much, we risk defying <em>Marks </em>v.<em> United States</em>.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 48" name="FRopinion1.48" href="#FNopinion1.48">48</a></sup>  According to <em>Marks</em>, when "a fragmented Court decides a case and no single rationale explaining the result enjoys the assent of five Justices, 'the holding of the Court may be viewed as that position taken by those Members who concurred in the judgments on the narrowest grounds.'&nbsp;"<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 49" name="FRopinion1.49" href="#FNopinion1.49">49</a></sup>  But notice that the dissent never actually gets around to telling us which opinion in <em>Apodaca</em> it considers to be the narrowest and controlling one under <em>Marks</em>--or why.  So while the dissent worries that we defy a <em>Marks</em> precedent, it is oddly coy about where exactly that precedent might be found.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The parties recognize what the dissent does not:  <em>Marks</em> has nothing to do with this case.  Unlike a <em>Marks</em> dispute where the litigants duel over which opinion represents the narrowest and controlling one, the parties before us accept that <em>Apodaca</em> yielded no controlling opinion at all.  In particular, both sides admit that Justice Powell's opinion cannot bind us--precisely because he relied on a dual-track rule of incorporation that an unbroken line of majority opinions before and after <em>Apodaca</em> has rejected.  Still, the dissent presses the issue, suggesting that a single Justice's opinion <em>can</em> overrule prior precedents under "the logic" of <em>Marks</em>.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 50" name="FRopinion1.50" href="#FNopinion1.50">50</a></sup>  But, as the dissent itself implicitly acknowledges, <em>Marks</em> never sought to offer or defend such a rule.  And, as we have seen, too, a rule like that would do more to harm than advance <em>stare decisis</em>.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The dissent's backup argument fares no better.  In the end, even the dissent is forced to concede that Justice Powell's <em>reasoning</em> in <em>Apodaca</em> lacks controlling force.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 51" name="FRopinion1.51" href="#FNopinion1.51">51</a></sup>  So far, so good.  But then the dissent suggests <em>Apodaca</em> somehow still manages to supply a controlling precedent as to its <em>result</em>.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 52" name="FRopinion1.52" href="#FNopinion1.52">52</a></sup>  Look closely, though.  The dissent's account of <em>Apodaca</em>'s <em>result</em> looks suspiciously like the <em>reasoning</em> of Justice Powell's opinion:  "In <em>Apodaca, </em>this means that when (1) a defendant is convicted in state court, (2) at least 10 of the 12 jurors vote to convict, and (3) the defendant argues that the conviction violates the Constitution because the vote was not unanimous, the challenge fails."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 53" name="FRopinion1.53" href="#FNopinion1.53">53</a></sup>  Where does the convenient "state court" qualification come from?  Neither the <em>Apodaca</em> plurality nor the dissent included any limitation like that--their opinions turned on the meaning of the Sixth Amendment.  What the dissent characterizes as <em>Apodaca</em>'s result turns out to be nothing more than Justice Powell's reasoning about dual-track incorporation dressed up to look like a logical proof.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;All of this does no more than highlight an old truth.  It is usually a judicial decision's reasoning--its <em>ratio</em> <em>decidendi</em>--that allows it to have life and effect in the disposition of future cases.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 54" name="FRopinion1.54" href="#FNopinion1.54">54</a></sup><sup>  </sup>As this Court has repeatedly explained in the context of summary affirmances, "&nbsp;'unexplicated'&nbsp;" decisions may "&nbsp;'settl[e] the issues for the parties, [but they are] not to be read as a renunciation by this Court of doctrines previously announced in our opinions.'&nbsp;"<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 55" name="FRopinion1.55" href="#FNopinion1.55">55</a></sup>  Much the same may be said here.  <em>Apodaca</em>'s judgment line resolved that case for the parties in that case.  It is binding in that sense.  But stripped from any reasoning, its judgment alone cannot be read to repudiate this Court's repeated pre-existing teachings on the Sixth and Fourteenth Amendments.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 56" name="FRopinion1.56" href="#FNopinion1.56">56</a></sup></p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">B</p>
</h2><h3><p align="CENTER">1</p>
</h3><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;There's another obstacle the dissent must overcome. Even if we accepted the premise that <em>Apodaca</em> established a precedent, no one on the Court today is prepared to say it was rightly decided, and <em>stare decisis </em>isn't supposed to be the art of methodically ignoring what everyone knows to be true.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 57" name="FRopinion1.57" href="#FNopinion1.57">57</a></sup>  Of course, the precedents of this Court warrant our deep respect as embodying the considered views of those who have come before.  But <em>stare decisis</em> has never been treated as "an inexorable command."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 58" name="FRopinion1.58" href="#FNopinion1.58">58</a></sup>  And the doctrine is "at its weakest when we interpret the Constitution"<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 59" name="FRopinion1.59" href="#FNopinion1.59">59</a></sup> because a mistaken judicial interpretation of that supreme law is often "practically impossible" to correct through other means.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 60" name="FRopinion1.60" href="#FNopinion1.60">60</a></sup>  To balance these considerations, when it revisits a precedent this Court has traditionally considered "the quality of the decision's reasoning; its consistency with related decisions; legal developments since the decision; and reliance on the decision."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 61" name="FRopinion1.61" href="#FNopinion1.61">61</a></sup>  In this case, each factor points in the same direction.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Start with the quality of the reasoning.  Whether we look to the plurality opinion or Justice Powell's separate concurrence, <em>Apodaca</em> was gravely mistaken; again, no Member of the Court today defends either as rightly decided.  Without repeating what we've already explained in detail, it's just an implacable fact that the plurality spent almost no time grappling with the historical meaning of the Sixth Amendment's jury trial right, this Court's long-repeated statements that it demands unanimity, or the racist origins of Louisiana's and Oregon's laws.  Instead, the plurality subjected the Constitution's jury trial right to an incomplete functionalist analysis of its own creation for which it spared one paragraph.  And, of course, five Justices expressly rejected the plurality's conclusion that the Sixth Amendment does not require unanimity.  Meanwhile, Justice Powell refused to follow this Court's incorporation precedents.  Nine Justices (including Justice Powell) recognized this for what it was; eight called it an error.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Looking to <em>Apodaca</em>'s consistency with related decisions and recent legal developments compounds the reasons for concern.  <em>Apodaca</em> sits uneasily with 120 years of preceding case law.  Given how unmoored it was from the start, it might seem unlikely that later developments could have done more to undermine the decision.  Yet they have.  While Justice Powell's dual-track theory of incorporation was already foreclosed in 1972, some at that time still argued that it might have a role to play outside the realm of criminal procedure.  Since then, the Court has held otherwise.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 62" name="FRopinion1.62" href="#FNopinion1.62">62</a></sup>  Until recently, dual-track incorporation attracted at least a measure of support in dissent.  But this Court has now roundly rejected it.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 63" name="FRopinion1.63" href="#FNopinion1.63">63</a></sup>  Nor has the plurality's rejection of the Sixth Amendment's historical unanimity requirement aged more gracefully.  As we've seen, in the years since <em>Apodaca</em>, this Court has spoken inconsistently about its meaning--but nonetheless referred to the traditional unanimity requirement on at least eight occasions.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 64" name="FRopinion1.64" href="#FNopinion1.64">64</a></sup>  In light of all this, calling <em>Apodaca</em> an outlier would be perhaps too suggestive of the possibility of company.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;When it comes to reliance interests, it's notable that neither Louisiana nor Oregon claims anything like the prospective economic, regulatory, or social disruption litigants seeking to preserve precedent usually invoke.  No one, it seems, has signed a contract, entered a marriage, purchased a home, or opened a business based on the expectation that, should a crime occur, at least the accused may be sent away by a 10-to-2 verdict.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 65" name="FRopinion1.65" href="#FNopinion1.65">65</a></sup>  Nor does anyone suggest that nonunanimous verdicts have "become part of our national culture."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 66" name="FRopinion1.66" href="#FNopinion1.66">66</a></sup>  It would be quite surprising if they had, given that nonunanimous verdicts are insufficient to convict in 48 States and federal court.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Instead, the only reliance interests that might be asserted here fall into two categories.  The first concerns the fact Louisiana and Oregon may need to retry defendants convicted of felonies by nonunanimous verdicts whose cases are still pending on direct appeal.  The dissent claims that this fact supplies the winning argument for retaining <em>Apodaca</em> because it has generated "enormous reliance interests" and overturning the case would provoke a "crushing" "tsunami" of follow-on litigation.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 67" name="FRopinion1.67" href="#FNopinion1.67">67</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The overstatement may be forgiven as intended for dramatic effect, but prior convictions in only two States are potentially affected by our judgment.  Those States credibly claim that the number of nonunanimous felony convictions still on direct appeal are somewhere in the hundreds,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 68" name="FRopinion1.68" href="#FNopinion1.68">68</a></sup> and retrying or plea bargaining these cases will surely impose a cost.  But new rules of criminal procedures usually do, often affecting significant numbers of pending cases across the whole country.  For example, after <em>Booker </em>v. <em>United States</em> held that the Federal Sentencing Guidelines must be advisory rather than mandatory, this Court vacated and remanded nearly 800 decisions to the courts of appeals. <em> </em>Similar consequences likely followed when <em>Crawford </em>v. <em>Washington</em> overturned prior interpretations of the Confrontation Clause<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 69" name="FRopinion1.69" href="#FNopinion1.69">69</a></sup> or <em>Arizona </em>v. <em>Gant </em>changed the law for searches incident to arrests.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 70" name="FRopinion1.70" href="#FNopinion1.70">70</a></sup>  Our decision here promises to cause less, and certainly nothing before us supports the dissent's surmise that it will cause wildly more, disruption than these other decisions.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">2</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The second and related reliance interest the dissent seizes upon involves the interest Louisiana and Oregon have in the security of their final criminal judgments.  In light of our decision today, the dissent worries that defendants whose appeals are already complete might seek to challenge their nonunanimous convictions through collateral (<em>i.e.,</em> habeas) review.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;But again the worries outstrip the facts.  Under <em>Teague </em>v.<em> Lane</em>, newly recognized rules of criminal procedure do not normally apply in collateral review.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 71" name="FRopinion1.71" href="#FNopinion1.71">71</a></sup>  True, <em>Teague </em>left open the possibility of an exception for "watershed rules" "implicat[ing] the fundamental fairness [and accuracy] of the trial."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 72" name="FRopinion1.72" href="#FNopinion1.72">72</a></sup>  But, as this language suggests, <em>Teague</em>'s test is a demanding one, so much so that this Court has yet to announce a new rule of criminal procedure capable of meeting it.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 73" name="FRopinion1.73" href="#FNopinion1.73">73</a></sup>  And the test is demanding by design, expressly calibrated to address the reliance interests States have in the finality of their criminal judgments.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 74" name="FRopinion1.74" href="#FNopinion1.74">74</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Nor is the <em>Teague</em> question even before us.  Whether the right to jury unanimity applies to cases on collateral review is a question for a future case where the parties will have a chance to brief the issue and we will benefit from their adversarial presentation.  That litigation is sure to come, and will rightly take into account the States' interest in the finality of their criminal convictions.  In this way, <em>Teague</em> frees us to say what we know to be true about the rights of the accused under our Constitution today, while leaving questions about the reliance interest States possess in their final judgments for later proceedings crafted to account for them.  It would hardly make sense to ignore that two-step process and count the State's reliance interests in final judgments both here and again there.  Certainly the dissent cites no authority for such double counting.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Instead, the dissent suggests that the feeble reliance interests it identifies should get a boost because the right to a unanimous jury trial has "little practical importance going forward."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 75" name="FRopinion1.75" href="#FNopinion1.75">75</a></sup>  In the dissent's telling, Louisiana has "abolished" nonunanimous verdicts and Oregon "seemed on the verge of doing the same until the Court intervened."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 76" name="FRopinion1.76" href="#FNopinion1.76">76</a></sup>  But, as the dissent itself concedes, a ruling for Louisiana would invite other States to relax their own unanimity requirements.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 77" name="FRopinion1.77" href="#FNopinion1.77">77</a></sup>  In fact, 14 jurisdictions have already told us that they would value the right to "experiment" with nonunanimous juries.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 78" name="FRopinion1.78" href="#FNopinion1.78">78</a></sup>  Besides, Louisiana's law bears only prospective effect, so the State continues to allow nonunanimous verdicts for crimes committed before 2019.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 79" name="FRopinion1.79" href="#FNopinion1.79">79</a></sup>  And while the dissent speculates that our grant of certiorari contributed to the failure of legal reform efforts in Oregon, its citation does not support its surmise.  No doubt, too, those who risk being subjected to nonunanimous juries in Louisiana and Oregon today, and elsewhere tomorrow, would dispute the dissent's suggestion that their Sixth Amendment rights are of "little practical importance."</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;That point suggests another.  In its valiant search for reliance interests, the dissent somehow misses maybe the most important one:  the reliance interests of the American people.  Taken at its word, the dissent would have us discard a Sixth Amendment right in perpetuity rather than ask two States to retry a slice of their prior criminal cases.  Whether that slice turns out to be large or small, it cannot outweigh the interest we all share in the preservation of our constitutionally promised liberties.  Indeed, the dissent can cite no case in which the one-time need to retry defendants has <em>ever </em>been sufficient to inter a constitutional right  forever.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In the final accounting, the dissent's <em>stare decisis</em> arguments round to zero.  We have an admittedly mistaken decision, on a constitutional issue, an outlier on the day it was decided, one that's become lonelier with time.  In arguing otherwise, the dissent must elide the reliance the American people place in their constitutionally protected liberties, overplay the competing interests of two States, count some of those interests twice, and make no small amount of new precedent all its own.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">V</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;On what ground would anyone have us leave Mr. Ramos in prison for the rest of his life?  Not a single Member of this Court is prepared to say Louisiana secured his conviction constitutionally under the Sixth Amendment.  No one before us suggests that the error was harmless.  Louisiana does not claim precedent commands an affirmance.  In the end, the best anyone can seem to muster against Mr. Ramos is that, if we dared to admit in his case what we all know to be true about the Sixth Amendment, we might have to say the same in some others.  But where is the justice in that?  Every judge must learn to live with the fact he or she will make some mistakes; it comes with the territory.  But it is something else entirely to perpetuate something we all know to be wrong only because we fear the consequences of being right.  The judgment of the Court of Appeals is</p>
<p align="RIGHT">Reversed.</p>
<p></p><hr>





<p align="CENTER"><span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor, J.</small></span>, concurring in part</p>
<p align="CENTER"> 590 U.&nbsp;S. ____ (2020)</p>


<p align="CENTER">No. 18-5924</p>


<center><h2><p align="CENTER">EVANGELISTO RAMOS, PETITIONER <em>v.</em> LOUISIANA</p>
</h2></center><center><h4><p align="CENTER"><span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>on writ of certiorari to the court of appeal</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>of louisiana, fourth circuit</small></span></p>
</h4></center><center><p align="CENTER">[April 20, 2020]</p>
</center><p></p><hr>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Sotomayor</small></span>, concurring as to all but Part IV-A.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I agree with most of the Court's rationale, and so I join all but Part IV-A of its opinion.  I write separately, however, to underscore three points.  First, overruling precedent here is not only warranted, but compelled.  Second, the interests at stake point far more clearly to that outcome than those in other recent cases.  And finally, the racially biased origins of the Louisiana and Oregon laws uniquely matter here.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">I</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Both the majority and the dissent rightly emphasize that <em>stare decisis</em> "has been a fundamental part of our jurisprudence since the founding."  <em>Post</em>,<em> </em>at<em> </em>12 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Alito, J</small></span>.); see<em> ante</em>, at 20.  Indeed, "[w]e generally adhere to our prior decisions, even if we question their soundness, because doing so 'promotes the evenhanded, predictable, and consistent development of legal principles, fosters reliance on judicial decisions, and contributes to the actual and perceived integrity of the judicial process.'&nbsp;"  <em>Alleyne</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 570 U.&nbsp;S. 99, 118 (2013) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor</small></span>, J., concurring) (quoting <em>Payne</em> v. <em>Tennessee</em>, 501 U.&nbsp;S. 808, 827 (1991)).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;But put simply, this is not a case where we cast aside precedent "simply because a majority of this Court now disagrees with" it.  <em>Alleyne</em>, 570 U.&nbsp;S., at 133 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Alito</small></span>, J., dissenting).  Rather, <em>Apodaca</em> v. <em>Oregon, </em>406 U.&nbsp;S. 464 (1972), was on shaky ground from the start.  That was not because of the functionalist analysis of that Court's plurality: Reasonable minds have disagreed over time--and continue to disagree--about the best mode of constitutional interpretation.  That the plurality in <em>Apodaca </em>used different interpretive tools from the majority here is not a reason on its own to discard precedent.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;What matters instead is that, as the majority rightly stresses, <em>Apodaca</em> is a universe of one--an opinion uniquely irreconcilable with not just one, but two, strands of constitutional precedent well established both before and after the decision.  The Court has long recognized that the Sixth Amendment requires unanimity.  <em>Ante,</em> at 11, 20-22.  Five Justices in <em>Apodaca </em>itself disagreed with that plurality's contrary view of the Sixth Amendment.  Justice Powell's theory of dual-track incorporation also fared no better: He recognized that his argument on that score came "late in the day."  <em>Johnson</em> v. <em>Louisiana</em>, 406 U.&nbsp;S. 356, 375 (1972) (concurring opinion).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Moreover, "[t]he force of <em>stare decisis</em> is at its nadir in cases concerning [criminal] procedur[e] rules that implicate fundamental constitutional protections."  <em>Alleyne</em>, 570 U.&nbsp;S., at 116, n.&nbsp;5.  And the constitutional protection here ranks among the most essential: the right to put the State to its burden, in a jury trial that comports with the Sixth Amendment, before facing criminal punishment.  See <em>Codispoti</em> v. <em>Pennsylvania</em>, 418 U.&nbsp;S. 506, 515-516 (1974) ("The Sixth Amendment represents a deep commitment of the Nation to the right of jury trial in serious criminal cases as a defense against arbitrary law enforcement" (internal quotation marks omitted)).  Where the State's power to imprison those like Ramos rests on an erroneous interpretation of the jury-trial right, the Court should not hesitate to reconsider its precedents.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">II</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In contrast to the criminal-procedure context, "[c]onsiderations in favor of <em>stare decisis</em> are at their acme in cases involving property and contract rights."  <em>Payne</em>,<em> </em>501 U. S., at 828.  Despite that fact, the Court has recently overruled precedent where the Court's shift threatened vast regulatory and economic consequences.  <em>Janus</em> v. <em>State, County, and Municipal Employees</em>, 585 U.&nbsp;S. ___ (2018); <em>id</em>., at ___ (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kagan</small></span>, J., dissenting) (slip op., at 23) (noting that the Court's opinion called into question "thousands of .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. contracts covering millions of workers"); see <em>South Dakota</em> v. <em>Wayfair, Inc.</em>, 585 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2018) (slip op., at 21) (noting the "legitimate" burdens that the Court's overruling of precedent would place on vendors who had started businesses in reliance on a previous decision).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;This case, by contrast, threatens no broad upheaval of private economic rights.  Particularly when compared to the interests of private parties who have structured their affairs in reliance on our decisions, the States' interests here in avoiding a modest number of retrials--emphasized at such length by the dissent--are much less weighty.  They are certainly not new: Opinions that force changes in a State's criminal procedure typically impose such costs.  And were this Court to take the dissent's approach--defending criminal-procedure opinions as wrong as <em>Apodaca</em> simply to avoid burdening criminal justice systems--it would never correct its criminal jurisprudence at all.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;To pick up on the majority's point, <em>ante</em>, at 23, in that alternate universe, a trial judge alone could still decide the critical facts necessary to sentence a defendant to death.  <em>Walton</em> v. <em>Arizona</em>, 497 U.&nbsp;S. 639 (1990), overruled by<em> Ring</em> v. <em>Arizona</em>, 536 U.&nbsp;S. 584 (2002).  An officer would still be able to search a car upon the arrest of any one of its recent occupants.  <em>New York</em> v. <em>Belton</em>, 453 U.&nbsp;S. 454 (1981), holding limited by <em>Arizona</em> v. <em>Gant</em>, 556 U.&nbsp;S. 332 (2009).  And States could still deprive a defendant of the right to confront her accuser so long as the incriminating statement was "reliable."  <em>Ohio</em> v. <em>Roberts</em>, 448 U.&nbsp;S. 56 (1980), abrogated by <em>Crawford</em> v. <em>Washington</em>, 541 U.&nbsp;S. 36 (2004).  The Constitution demands more than the continued use of flawed criminal procedures--all because the Court fears the consequences of changing course.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">III</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Finally, the majority vividly describes the legacy of racism that generated Louisiana's and Oregon's laws.  <em>Ante,</em> at 1-2, 13-14, and n. 44.  Although Ramos does not bring an equal protection challenge, the history is worthy of this Court's attention.  That is not simply because that legacy existed in the first place--unfortunately, many laws and policies in this country have had some history of racial animus--but also because the States' legislatures never truly grappled with the laws' sordid history in reenacting them.  See generally <em>United States</em> v. <em>Fordice</em>, 505 U.&nbsp;S. 717, 729 (1992) (policies that are "traceable" to a State's <em>de jure</em> racial segregation and that still "have discriminatory effects" offend the Equal Protection Clause).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Where a law otherwise is untethered to racial bias--and perhaps also where a legislature actually confronts a law's tawdry past in reenacting it--the new law may well be free of discriminatory taint.  That cannot be said of the laws at issue here.  While the dissent points to the "legitimate" reasons for Louisiana's reenactment, <em>post</em>,<em> </em>at 3-4, Louisiana's perhaps only effort to contend with the law's discriminatory purpose and effects came recently, when the law was repealed altogether.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Today, Louisiana's and Oregon's laws are fully--and rightly--relegated to the dustbin of history.  And so, too, is <em>Apodaca</em>.  While overruling precedent must be rare, this Court should not shy away from correcting its errors where the right to avoid imprisonment pursuant to unconstitutional procedures hangs in the balance.</p>
<p></p><hr>




<p align="CENTER"><span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kavanaugh, J.</small></span>, concurring in part</p>
<p align="CENTER"> 590 U.&nbsp;S. ____ (2020)</p>


<p align="CENTER">No. 18-5924</p>


<center><h2><p align="CENTER">EVANGELISTO RAMOS, PETITIONER <em>v.</em> LOUISIANA</p>
</h2></center><center><h4><p align="CENTER"><span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>on writ of certiorari to the court of appeal</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>of louisiana, fourth circuit</small></span></p>
</h4></center><center><p align="CENTER">[April 20, 2020]</p>
</center><p></p><hr>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kavanaugh</small></span>, concurring in part.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In <em>Apodaca </em>v. <em>Oregon</em>, this Court held that state juries need not be unanimous in order to convict a criminal defendant.  406 U.&nbsp;S. 404 (1972).  Two States, Louisiana and Oregon, have continued to use non-unanimous juries in criminal cases.  Today, the Court overrules <em>Apodaca </em>and holds that state juries must be unanimous in order to convict a criminal defendant<em>.</em></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I agree with the Court that the time has come to overrule <em>Apodaca</em>.  I therefore join the introduction and Parts I, II-A, III, and IV-B-1 of the Court's persuasive and important opinion.  I write separately to explain my view of how <em>stare decisis</em> applies to this case.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">I</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The legal doctrine of <em>stare decisis</em> derives from the Latin maxim "<em>stare decisis</em> <em>et non quieta movere</em>," which means to stand by the thing decided and not disturb the calm.  The doctrine reflects respect for the accumulated wisdom of judges who have previously tried to solve the same problem.  In 1765, Blackstone--"the preeminent authority on English law for the founding generation," <em>Alden </em>v. <em>Maine</em>, 527 U.&nbsp;S. 706, 715 (1999)--wrote that "it is an established rule to abide by former precedents," to "keep the scale of justice even and steady, and not liable to waver with every new judge's opinion."  1 W. Blackstone, Commentaries on the Laws of England 69 (1765).  The Framers of our Constitution understood that the doctrine of <em>stare decisis</em> is part of the "judicial Power" and rooted in Article III of the Constitution.  Writing in Federalist 78, Alexander Hamilton emphasized the importance of <em>stare decisis</em>: To "avoid an arbitrary discretion in the courts, it is indispensable" that federal judges "should be bound down by strict rules and precedents, which serve to define and point out their duty in every particular case that comes before them."  The Federalist No. 78, p. 529 (J. Cooke ed. 1961).  In the words of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>The</small></span> <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Chief Justice</small></span>, <em>stare decisis</em>' "greatest purpose is to serve a constitutional ideal--the rule of law."  <em>Citizens United</em> v. <em>Federal Election Comm'n</em>, 558 U.&nbsp;S. 310, 378 (2010) (concurring opinion).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;This Court has repeatedly explained that<em> stare decisis</em> "promotes the evenhanded, predictable, and consistent development of legal principles, fosters reliance on judicial decisions, and contributes to the actual and perceived integrity of the judicial process."  <em>Payne</em> v. <em>Tennessee</em>, 501 U.&nbsp;S. 808, 827 (1991).  The doctrine "permits society to presume that bedrock principles are founded in the law rather than in the proclivities of individuals, and thereby contributes to the integrity of our constitutional system of government, both in appearance and in fact."  <em>Vasquez</em> v. <em>Hillery</em>, 474 U.&nbsp;S. 254, 265-266 (1986).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The doctrine of <em>stare decisis</em> does not mean, of course, that the Court should never overrule erroneous precedents.  All Justices now on this Court agree that it is sometimes appropriate for the Court to overrule erroneous decisions.  Indeed, in just the last few Terms, every current Member of this Court has voted to overrule multiple constitutional precedents.  See, <em>e.g., Knick</em> v. <em>Township of Scott</em>, 588 U.&nbsp;S. ___ (2019); <em>Franchise Tax Bd. of Cal.</em> v. <em>Hyatt</em>, 587 U.&nbsp;S. ___ (2019); <em>Janus</em> v. <em>State, County, and Municipal Employees</em>, 585 U.&nbsp;S. ___ (2018); <em>Hurst</em> v. <em>Florida</em>, 577 U.&nbsp;S. ___ (2016); <em>Obergefell</em> v. <em>Hodges</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S. 644 (2015); <em>Johnson</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S. 591 (2015); <em>Alleyne</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 570 U.&nbsp;S. 99 (2013); see also Baude, Precedent and Discretion, 2020 S.&nbsp;Ct. Rev. 1, 4 (forthcoming) ("Nobody on the Court believes in absolute stare decisis").</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Historically, moreover, some of the Court's most notable and consequential decisions have entailed overruling precedent.  See, <em>e.g., Obergefell</em> v. <em>Hodges</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S. 644 (2015);<em> Citizens United</em> v. <em>Federal Election Comm'n</em>, 558 U.&nbsp;S. 310 (2010); <em>Montejo</em> v. <em>Louisiana</em>, 556 U.&nbsp;S. 778 (2009); <em>Crawford</em> v. <em>Washington</em>, 541 U.&nbsp;S. 36 (2004); <em>Lawrence</em> v. <em>Texas</em>, 539 U.&nbsp;S. 558 (2003); <em>Ring</em> v. <em>Arizona</em>, 536 U.&nbsp;S. 584 (2002); <em>Agostini</em> v. <em>Felton</em>, 521 U.&nbsp;S. 203 (1997); <em>Seminole Tribe of Fla.</em> v. <em>Florida</em>, 517 U.&nbsp;S. 44 (1996); <em>Planned Parenthood of Southeastern Pa.</em> v. <em>Casey</em>, 505 U.&nbsp;S. 833 (1992);<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 1" name="FRopinion1.1" href="#FNopinion1.1">1</a></sup> <em>Payne</em> v. <em>Tennessee</em>, 501 U.&nbsp;S. 808 (1991); <em>Batson</em> v. <em>Kentucky</em>, 476 U.&nbsp;S. 79 (1986); <em>Garcia</em> v. <em>San Antonio Metropolitan Transit Authority</em>, 469 U.&nbsp;S. 528 (1985); <em>Illinois</em> v. <em>Gates</em>, 462 U.&nbsp;S. 213 (1983); <em>United States</em> v. <em>Scott</em>, 437 U.&nbsp;S. 82 (1978); <em>Craig</em> v. <em>Boren</em>, 429 U.&nbsp;S. 190 (1976); <em>Taylor</em> v. <em>Louisiana</em>, 419 U.&nbsp;S. 522 (1975); <em>Brandenburg</em> v. <em>Ohio</em>, 395 U.&nbsp;S. 444 (1969) (<em>per curiam</em>); <em>Katz</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 389 U.&nbsp;S. 347 (1967); <em>Miranda</em> v. <em>Arizona</em>, 384 U.&nbsp;S. 436 (1966); <em>Malloy</em> v. <em>Hogan</em>, 378 U.&nbsp;S. 1 (1964); <em>Wesberry</em> v. <em>Sanders</em>, 376 U.&nbsp;S. 1 (1964); <em>Gideon</em> v. <em>Wainwright</em>, 372 U.&nbsp;S. 335 (1963); <em>Baker</em> v. <em>Carr</em>, 369 U.&nbsp;S. 186 (1962); <em>Mapp</em> v. <em>Ohio</em>, 367 U.&nbsp;S. 643 (1961); <em>Brown </em>v. <em>Board of Education</em>,<em> </em>347 U.&nbsp;S. 483 (1954); <em>Smith</em> v. <em>Allwright</em>, 321 U.&nbsp;S. 649 (1944); <em>West Virginia Bd. of Ed.</em> v. <em>Barnette</em>, 319 U.&nbsp;S. 624 (1943); <em>United States</em> v. <em>Darby</em>, 312 U.&nbsp;S. 100 (1941); <em>Erie R. Co.</em> v. <em>Tompkins</em>, 304 U.&nbsp;S. 64 (1938); <em>West Coast Hotel Co.</em> v. <em>Parrish</em>, 300 U.&nbsp;S. 379 (1937).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The lengthy and extraordinary list of landmark cases that overruled precedent includes the single most important and greatest decision in this Court's history, <em>Brown</em> v. <em>Board of Education</em>, which repudiated the separate but equal doctrine of <em>Plessy</em> v. <em>Ferguson</em>, 163 U.&nbsp;S. 537 (1896).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;As those many examples demonstrate, the doctrine of <em>stare decisis</em> does not dictate, and no one seriously maintains, that the Court should <em>never </em>overrule erroneous precedent.  As the Court has often stated and repeats today, <em>stare decisis</em> is not an "inexorable command."  <em>E.g., ante,</em>  at 20.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;On the other hand, as Justice Jackson explained, just "because one should avoid Scylla is no reason for crashing into Charybdis." Jackson, Decisional Law and Stare Decisis, 30 A.&nbsp;B.&nbsp;A. J. 334 (1944).  So no one advocates that the Court should <em>always </em>overrule erroneous precedent.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Rather, applying the doctrine of <em>stare decisis</em>, this Court ordinarily adheres to precedent, but <em>sometimes </em>overrules precedent.  The difficult question, then, is when to overrule an erroneous precedent.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;To begin with, the Court's precedents on precedent distinguish statutory cases from constitutional cases.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In statutory cases, <em>stare decisis</em> is comparatively strict, as history shows and the Court has often stated.  That is because Congress and the President can alter a statutory precedent by enacting new legislation.  To be sure, enacting new legislation requires finding room in a crowded legislative docket and securing the agreement of the House, the Senate (in effect, 60 Senators), and the President.  Both by design and as a matter of fact, enacting new legislation is difficult--and far more difficult than the Court's cases sometimes seem to assume.  Nonetheless, the Court has ordinarily left the updating or correction of erroneous statutory precedents to the legislative process.  See, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>Kimble</em> v. <em>Marvel Entertainment, LLC</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S. 446, 456-457 (2015); <em>Patterson</em> v. <em>McLean Credit Union</em>, 491 U.&nbsp;S. 164, 172-173 (1989); <em>Flood</em> v. <em>Kuhn</em>, 407 U.&nbsp;S. 258, 283-284 (1972).  The principle that "it is more important that the applicable rule of law be settled than that it be settled right" is "commonly true even where the error is a matter of serious concern, <em>provided correction can be had by legislation</em>."  <em>Burnet</em> v. <em>Coronado Oil &amp; Gas Co.</em>, 285 U.&nbsp;S. 393, 406 (1932) (Brandeis, J., dissenting) (emphasis added).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 2" name="FRopinion1.2" href="#FNopinion1.2">2</a></sup></p>
