Prisoners whose direct appeals have ended will argue that today's decision allows them to challenge their convictions on collateral review, and if those claims succeed, the courts of Louisiana and Oregon are almost sure to be overwhelmed.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The majority's response to this possibility is evasive.  It begins by hinting that today's decision will not apply on collateral review under the framework adopted in <em>Teague </em>v.<em> Lane</em>, 489 U.&nbsp;S. 288, 315 (1989) (plurality opinion).  Under <em>Teague</em>, "an old rule applies both on direct and collateral review," but if today's decision constitutes a new procedural rule, prisoners will be able to rely on it in a collateral proceeding only if it is what we have termed a "watershed rule" that implicates "the fundamental fairness and accuracy of the criminal proceeding."  <em>Whorton </em>v. <em>Bockting</em>, 549 U.&nbsp;S. 406, 416 (2007).  Noting that we have never found a new rule of criminal procedure to qualify as "watershed," the Court hints that the decision in this case is likely to meet the same fate.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;But having feinted in this direction, the Court quickly changes course and says that the application of today's decision to prisoners whose appeals have ended should not concern us.  <em>Ante</em>, at 23-24.  That question, we are told, will be decided in a later case.  <em>Ibid</em>.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The majority cannot have it both ways.  As long as retroactive application on collateral review remains a real possibility, the crushing burden that this would entail cannot be ignored.  And while it is true that this Court has been chary in recognizing new watershed rules, it is by no means clear that <em>Teague</em> will preclude the application of today's decision on collateral review.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Teague</em> applies only to a "new rule," and the positions taken by some in the majority may lead to the conclusion that the rule announced today is an old rule.  Take the proposition, adopted by three Members of the majority, that <em>Apodaca</em> was never a precedent.  Those Justices, along with the rest of the majority, take the position that our cases established well before <em>Apodaca </em>both that the Sixth Amendment requires unanimity, <em>ante</em>, at 6-7, and that it applies in the same way in state and federal court, <em>ante</em>, at 9.  Thus, if <em>Apodaca</em> was never a precedent and did not disturb what had previously been established, it may be argued that today's decision does not impose a new rule but instead merely recognizes what the correct rule has been for many years.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Two other Justices in the majority acknowledge that <em>Apodaca</em> was a precedent and thus would presumably regard today's decision as a "new rule," but the question remains whether today's decision qualifies as a "watershed rule."  <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kavanaugh</small></span> concludes that it does not and all but decides--without briefing or argument--that the decision will not apply retroactively on federal collateral review and similarly that there will be no successful claims of ineffective assistance of counsel for failing to challenge <em>Apodaca</em>.  See <em>ante,</em> at 15-17 (opinion concurring in part).</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The remaining Justices in the majority, and those of us in dissent, express no view on this question, but the majority's depiction of the unanimity requirement as a hallowed right that Louisiana and Oregon flouted for ignominious reasons certainly provides fuel for the argument that the rule announced today meets the test.  And in Oregon, the State most severely impacted by today's decision, watershed status may not matter since the State Supreme Court has reserved decision on whether state law gives prisoners a greater opportunity to invoke new precedents in state collateral proceedings.  See <em>Verduzco</em> v. <em>State</em>, 357 Ore. 553, 574, 355 P. 3d 902, 914 (2015).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 31" name="FRopinion1.31" href="#FNopinion1.31">31</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Whatever the ultimate resolution of the retroactivity question, the reliance here is not only massive; it is concrete.  Cf. <em>Dickerson </em>v. <em>United States</em>, 530 U.&nbsp;S. 428, 443 (2000) (reliance weighed heavily in favor of precedent simply because the warnings in <em>Miranda</em> v. <em>Arizona</em>, 384 U.&nbsp;S. 436, had become "part of our national culture").  In my view, it weighs decisively against overruling <em>Apodaca.</em></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In reaching this conclusion, I do not disregard the interests of petitioner and others who were convicted by a less-than-unanimous vote.  It is not accurate to imply that these defendants would have been spared conviction if unanimity had been required.  In many cases, if a unanimous vote had been needed, the jury would have continued to deliberate and the one or two holdouts might well have ultimately voted to convict.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 32" name="FRopinion1.32" href="#FNopinion1.32">32</a></sup>  This is almost certainly the situation in Oregon, where it is estimated that as many as two-thirds of all criminal trials have ended with a non-unanimous verdict.  See Brief for State of Oregon as <em>Amicus</em> <em>Curiae</em> 12.  It is impossible to believe that all these cases would have resulted in mistrials if unanimity had been demanded.  Instead, after a vote of 11 to 1 or 10 to 2, it is likely that deliberations would have continued and unanimity would have been achieved.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Nevertheless, the plight of defendants convicted by non-unanimous votes is important and cannot be overlooked, but that alone cannot be dispositive of the <em>stare decisis</em> question.  Otherwise, <em>stare decisis</em> would never apply in a case in which a criminal defendant challenges a precedent that led to conviction.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">D</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The reliance in this case far outstrips that asserted in recent cases in which past precedents were overruled.  Last Term, when we overturned two past decisions, there were strenuous dissents voicing fears about the future of <em>stare decisis</em>.  See <em>Franchise Tax Bd. of Cal. </em>v.<em> Hyatt</em>, 587 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2019) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Breyer,&nbsp;J.,</small></span> dissenting); <em>Knick </em>v.<em> Township of Scott</em>,<em> </em>588 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2019) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kagan,&nbsp;J.,</small></span> dissenting).  Yet in neither of those cases was there reliance like that present here.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In <em>Franchise Tax Board</em>, the dissent claimed only the airiest sort of reliance, the public's expectation that past  decisions would remain on the books.  587 U.&nbsp;S., at ___-___ (opinion of<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small> Breyer,&nbsp;J.</small></span>) (slip op., at 12-13).  And in <em>Knick</em>, the dissent disclaimed any reliance at all.  588 U.&nbsp;S., at ___ (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kagan,&nbsp;J.</small></span>) (slip op., at 17).  The same was true the year before in <em>South Dakota </em>v<em>. Wayfair</em>, <em>Inc</em>., 585 U.&nbsp;S. ___ (2018), where the dissent did not contend that any legitimate reliance interests weighed in favor of preserving the decision that the Court overruled.  <em>Id.</em>, at ___-___  (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Roberts, C.&nbsp;J.</small></span>) (slip op., at 1-2).  And our unanimous decision in <em>Pearson </em>v<em>. Callahan</em>, 555 U.&nbsp;S. 223, 233 (2009), found that no reliance interests were involved.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In other cases overruling prior decisions, the dissents claimed that reliance interests were at stake, but whatever one may think about the weight of those interests, no one can argue that they are comparable to those in this case.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In <em>Montejo </em>v.<em> Louisiana</em>,<em> </em>556 U.&nbsp;S. 778, 793-797 (2009), the Court abrogated a prophylactic rule that had been adopted in <em>Michigan </em>v.<em> Jackson</em>, 475 U.&nbsp;S. 625 (1986),  to protect a defendant's right to counsel during postarraignment interrogation.  The dissent did not claim that any defendants had relied on this rule, arguing instead that the public at large had an interest "in knowing that counsel, once secured, may be reasonably relied upon as a medium between the accused and the power of the State."  <em>Montejo</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 809 (opinion of Stevens<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>, J.</small></span>).  This abstract interest, if it can be called reliance in any proper sense of the term, is a far cry from what is at stake here.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In <em>Citizens United </em>v.<em> Federal Election Comm'n</em>, 558 U.&nbsp;S. 310 (2010), where we overruled precedent allowing laws that prohibited corporations' election-related speech, we found that "[n]o serious reliance interests" were implicated, <em>id.</em>, at 365, since the only reliance asserted by the dissent was the time and effort put in by federal and state lawmakers in adopting the provisions at issue, <em>id</em>., at 411-412 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>S</small></span>tevens<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>, J.,</small></span> concurring in part and dissenting in part).  In this case, by contrast, what is at stake is not the time and effort of Louisiana and Oregon lawmakers but a monumental litigation burden and the potential inability to retry cases that might well have ended with a unanimous verdict if that had been required.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Finally, in <em>Janus </em>v.<em> State, County, and Municipal Employees</em>, 585 U.&nbsp;S. ___ (2018)<em>, </em>where we overruled <em>Abood </em>v.<em> Detroit Bd. of Ed.</em>,<em> </em>431 U.&nbsp;S. 209 (1977), we carefully considered and addressed the question of reliance, and whatever one may think about the extent of the legitimate reliance in that case, it is not in the same league as that present here.  <em>Abood</em> had held that a public sector employer may require non-union members to pay a portion of the dues collected from union members.  431 U.&nbsp;S., at 235-236.  In overruling that decision, we acknowledged that existing labor contracts might have been negotiated in reliance on <em>Abood</em>, but we noted that most labor contracts are of short duration, that unions had been on notice for some time that the Court had serious misgivings about <em>Abood</em>, and that unions could have insisted on contractual provisions to protect their interests if <em>Abood</em> later fell.<em>  Janus</em>, <em>supra</em>, at ___-___ (slip op., at 44-47).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 33" name="FRopinion1.33" href="#FNopinion1.33">33</a></sup></p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;By striking down a precedent upon which there has been massive and entirely reasonable reliance, the majority sets an important precedent about <em>stare decisis</em>.  I assume that those in the majority will apply the same standard in future cases.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">*  *  *</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Under the approach to <em>stare decisis</em> that we have taken in recent years, <em>Apodaca </em>should not be overruled.  I would therefore affirm the judgment below, and I<em> </em>respectfully  dissent.</p>
<p></p><hr>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 15" name="FNopinion1.15" href="#FRopinion1.15">15</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;To be sure, a few of the Colonies had relaxed (and then restored) the unanimity requirement well before the founding.  For example, during a two decade period in the late 17th century, the Carolinas experimented with a non-common law system designed to encourage a feudal social structure; this "reactionary" constitution permitted conviction by majority vote.  See Carolina Const., Art. 69 (1669), in 5 Thorpe 2781; Reinsch, The English Common Law in the Early American Colonies, in 1 Select Essays in Anglo-American Legal History 407 (1907).  But, as Louisiana admits, by the time of the Sixth Amendment's adoption, unanimity had again become the accepted rule.  See Brief for Respondent 17.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 35" name="FNopinion1.35" href="#FRopinion1.35">35</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Gaudin</em>, 515 U.&nbsp;S., at 510; <em>Richardson</em>, 526 U.&nbsp;S., at 817; <em>Apprendi</em>, 530 U.&nbsp;S., at 477; <em>Southern Union Co.</em>, 567 U.&nbsp;S., at 356; <em>Blakely</em>, 542 U.&nbsp;S., at 301-302; <em>Booker</em>, 543 U.&nbsp;S., at 238; <em>Descamps</em>, 570 U.&nbsp;S., at 269; <em>Haymond</em>, 588 U.&nbsp;S., at ___-___ (plurality opinion) (slip op., at 6-7).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 36" name="FNopinion1.36" href="#FRopinion1.36">36</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>Burch</em> v. <em>Louisiana</em>, 441 U.&nbsp;S. 130, 136, and n.&nbsp;9 (1979) (describing both plurality opinion and Justice Powell's separate writing); <em>Brown</em> v. <em>Louisiana</em>, 447 U.&nbsp;S. 323, 331 (1980) (plurality opinion) (describing neither); see also <em>McKoy</em> v. <em>North Carolina</em>, 494 U.&nbsp;S. 433, 468 (1990) (Scalia, J., dissenting) (same).  On a few occasions we've suggested that perhaps <em>Apodaca </em>means the Sixth Amendment does not require unanimity at all.  See <em>Ludwig</em> v. <em>Massachusetts</em>, 427 U.&nbsp;S. 618, 625 (1976) (quoting <em>Apodaca</em> plurality); <em>Gaudin</em>, 515 U.&nbsp;S., at 510, n.&nbsp;2 (same); see also <em>Holland</em> v. <em>Illinois</em>, 493 U.&nbsp;S. 474, 511 (1990) (Stevens, J., dissenting) (same).  But on another occasion, we suggested that it could make a difference whether a particular right was rooted in the Sixth Amendment's jury trial guarantee or Fourteenth Amendment due process guarantee.  See <em>Schad</em> v. <em>Arizona</em>, 501 U.&nbsp;S. 624, 634, n.&nbsp;5 (1991) (plurality opinion).  The dissent contends that these cases have "reiterated time and again what <em>Apodaca</em> had established."  <em>Post</em>, at 6 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Alito, J</small></span>.).  More accurately, these "reiterations" have suggested different things at different times.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 37" name="FNopinion1.37" href="#FRopinion1.37">37</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Brief for Respondent 47; Tr. of Oral Arg. 37-38.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 38" name="FNopinion1.38" href="#FRopinion1.38">38</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In at least some of these cases, that may be a fair characterization.  For example, while <em>Thompson </em>was quick to say that the U.&nbsp;S. Constitution requires "the unanimous verdict of a jury of twelve persons," the question before the Court was whether, in the circumstances of the defendant's case, a trial by eight jurors in a Utah state court would violate the <em>Ex Post Facto</em> Clause.  170 U.&nbsp;S., at 351.  The Sixth Amendment's unanimity requirement was unnecessary to the outcome, and the Utah Constitution required unanimity either way.  <em>Id.</em>, at 345.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 39" name="FNopinion1.39" href="#FRopinion1.39">39</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;1 Annals of Cong. 435 (1789).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 40" name="FNopinion1.40" href="#FRopinion1.40">40</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In private writings, Madison did explain some of the Senate's objections with his original phrasing of the vicinage requirement.  See 5 Writings of James Madison 420-424 (G. Hunt ed. 1904) (letters to E. Pendleton, Sept. 14 and 23, 1789).  But this is little help in explaining the other changes made in the Senate.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 41" name="FNopinion1.41" href="#FRopinion1.41">41</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;406 U.&nbsp;S., at 410 (plurality opinion) (quoting <em>Williams </em>v.<em> Florida</em>, 399 U.&nbsp;S. 78, 100 (1970), and<em> Duncan</em>,<em> </em>391 U.&nbsp;S., at 156).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 42" name="FNopinion1.42" href="#FRopinion1.42">42</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;406 U.&nbsp;S., at<em> </em>410-411.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 43" name="FNopinion1.43" href="#FRopinion1.43">43</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<em>Id.,</em> at 411.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 44" name="FNopinion1.44" href="#FRopinion1.44">44</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The dissent chides us for acknowledging the racist history of Louisiana's and Oregon's laws, and commends the <em>Apodaca</em> plurality's decision to disregard these facts.  <em>Post,</em> at 2-5, 14.  But if the Sixth Amendment calls on judges to assess the functional benefits of jury rules, as the <em>Apodaca</em> plurality suggested, how can that analysis proceed to ignore the very functions those rules were adopted to serve?  The dissent answers that Louisiana and Oregon eventually recodified their nonunanimous jury laws in new proceedings untainted by racism.  See <em>post</em>, at 3-4, n. 3.<em>  </em>But that cannot explain <em>Apodaca</em>'s omission:  The States' proceedings took place only <em>after</em> the Court's decision.  Nor can our shared respect for "rational and civil discourse," <em>post</em>,<em> </em>at 5, supply an excuse for leaving an uncomfortable past unexamined.  Still, the dissent is right about one thing--a jurisdiction adopting a nonunanimous jury rule even for benign reasons would still violate the Sixth Amendment.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 45" name="FNopinion1.45" href="#FRopinion1.45">45</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See H. Kalven &amp; H. Zeisel, The American Jury 461 (1966); Diamond, Rose, &amp; Murphy, Revisiting the Unanimity Requirement:  The Behavior of the Nonunanimous Civil Jury, 100 Nw. U. L. Rev. 201, 207-208 (2006).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 46" name="FNopinion1.46" href="#FRopinion1.46">46</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Devine et al., Jury Decision Making:  45 Years of Empirical Research on Deliberating Groups, 7 Psych. Pub. Pol'y &amp; L. 622, 669 (2001); R. Hastie, S. Penrod, &amp; N. Pennington, Inside the Jury 115, 164-165 (1983); Hans, The Power of Twelve:  The Impact of Jury Size and Unanimity on Civil Jury Decision Making, 4 Del. L. Rev. 1, 24-25 (2001).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 47" name="FNopinion1.47" href="#FRopinion1.47">47</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The dissent seems to suggest that we must abandon the Sixth Amendment's historical meaning in favor of <em>Apodaca</em>'s functionalism because a parade of horribles would follow otherwise.  In particular, the dissent reminds us that, at points and places in our history, women were not permitted to sit on juries.  See <em>post</em>, at 15-16.  But we hardly need <em>Apodaca</em>'s functionalism to avoid repeating that wrong.  Unlike the rule of unanimity, rules about who qualified as a defendant's "peer" varied considerably at common law at the time of the Sixth Amendment's adoption.  Reflecting that fact, the Judiciary Act of 1789--adopted by the same Congress that passed the Sixth Amendment--initially pegged the qualifications for federal jury service to the relevant state jury qualification requirements.  1 Stat. 88.  As a result, for much of this Nation's early history the composition of federal juries varied both geographically and over time.  See Hickey, Federal Legislation: Improvement of the Jury System in Federal Courts, 35 Geo. L. J. 500, 506-507 (1947); <em>Taylor </em>v.<em> Louisiana</em>, 419 U.&nbsp;S. 522, 536 (1975).  Ultimately, however, the people themselves adopted further constitutional amendments that prohibit invidious discrimination.  So today the Sixth Amendment's promise of a jury of one's peers means a jury selected from a representative crosssection of the entire community.  See <em>Strauder</em>, 100 U.&nbsp;S., at 307-308; <em>Smith </em>v.<em> Texas</em>, 311 U.&nbsp;S. 128, 130 (1940); <em>Taylor</em>, 419 U.&nbsp;S., at 527.</p>
<p>Relatedly, the dissent suggests that, before doing anything here, we should survey all changes in jury practices since 1791.  See <em>post</em>, at 16, n.&nbsp;26.  It sounds like an interesting study--but not one that could alter the plain meaning of the Constitution or obliviate its undisputed unanimity requirement.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 48" name="FNopinion1.48" href="#FRopinion1.48">48</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;430 U.&nbsp;S. 188 (1977).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 56" name="FNopinion1.56" href="#FRopinion1.56">56</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The dissent floats a different theory when it suggests this Court's denials of certiorari in cases seeking to clarify <em>Apodaca</em> is evidence of <em>Apodaca</em>'s precedential force.  <em>Post,</em> at 7.  But "[t]he significance of a denial of a petition for certiorari ought no longer .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. require discussion.  This Court has said again and again and again that such a denial has no legal significance whatever bearing on the merits of the claim."  <em>Darr </em>v.<em> Burford</em>, 339 U.&nbsp;S. 200, 226 (1950) (Frankfurter, J., dissenting).</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In <em>Casey</em>, the Court reaffirmed what it described as the "central holding" of <em>Roe</em> v. <em>Wade</em>, 410 U.&nbsp;S. 113 (1973), the Court expressly rejected <em>Roe</em>'s trimester framework, and the Court expressly overruled two other important abortion precedents, <em>Akron</em> v. <em>Akron Center for Reproductive Health, Inc.</em>, 462 U.&nbsp;S. 416 (1983), and <em>Thornburgh</em> v. <em>American College of Obstetricians and Gynecologists</em>, 476 U.&nbsp;S. 747 (1986).  See <em>Casey</em>, 505 U.&nbsp;S., at 861; <em>id.,</em> at 870, 873 (plurality opinion).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court's precedents applying common-law statutes and pronouncing the Court's own interpretive methods and principles typically do not fall within that category of stringent statutory <em>stare decisis</em>.  See <em>Leegin Creative Leather Products, Inc.</em> v. <em>PSKS, Inc.</em>, 551 U.&nbsp;S. 877, 899-907 (2007); <em>Kisor</em> v. <em>Wilkie</em>, 588 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___-___ (2019) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Gorsuch</small></span>, J., concurring in judgment) (slip op., at 34-36).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court first used the term "special justification" in the <em>stare decisis</em> context in 1984, without explaining what the term might entail.  See <em>Arizona</em> v. <em>Rumsey</em>, 467 U.&nbsp;S. 203, 212.  In employing that term, the Court did not suggest that it was imposing a new <em>stare decisis</em> requirement as opposed to merely describing the Court's historical practice with respect to <em>stare decisis</em>.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Another important factor that limits the number of overrulings is that the Court typically does not overrule a precedent unless a party requests overruling, or at least unless the Court receives briefing and argument on the <em>stare decisis</em> question.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNopinion1.5" href="#FRopinion1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;To be clear, the <em>stare decisis</em> issue in this case is one of horizontal <em>stare decisis</em>--that is, the respect that this Court owes to its own precedents and the circumstances under which this Court may appropriately overrule a precedent.  By contrast, vertical <em>stare decisis</em> is absolute, as it must be in a hierarchical system with "one supreme Court."  U.&nbsp;S. Const., Art III, §1.  In other words, the state courts and the other federal courts have a constitutional obligation to follow a precedent of this Court unless and until it is overruled by this Court.  See <em>Rodriguez de Quijas</em> v. <em>Shearson/American Express, Inc.</em>, 490 U.&nbsp;S. 477, 484 (1989).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNopinion1.6" href="#FRopinion1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Notwithstanding the splintered 4-1-4 decision in <em>Apodaca</em>, its bottomline result carried precedential force.<em>  </em>In the American system of <em>stare decisis</em>, the result and the reasoning each independently have precedential force, and courts are therefore bound to follow both the result and the reasoning of a prior decision.  See <em>Seminole Tribe of Fla.</em> v. <em>Florida</em>, 517 U.&nbsp;S. 44, 67 (1996); <em>Randall</em> v. <em>Sorrell</em>, 548 U.&nbsp;S. 230, 243 (2006) (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Breyer</small></span>, J.); <em>County of Allegheny</em> v. <em>American Civil Liberties Union, Greater Pittsburgh Chapter</em>, 492 U.&nbsp;S. 573, 668 (1989) (Kennedy, J., concurring in judgment in part and dissenting in part).  The result of <em>Apodaca </em>was that state criminal juries need not be unanimous.  That precedential result has been followed by this Court and the other federal and state courts for the last 48 years.  To be sure, <em>Apodaca </em>had no majority opinion.  When the Court's decision is splintered, courts follow the result, and they also follow the reasoning or standards set forth in the opinion constituting the "narrowest grounds" of the Justices in the majority.  See <em>Marks</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 430 U.&nbsp;S. 188, 193 (1977).  That <em>Marks </em>rule is ordinarily commonsensical to apply and usually means that courts in essence heed the opinion that occupies the middle-ground position between (i) the broadest opinion among the Justices in the majority and (ii)&nbsp;the dissenting opinion.  See <em>United States </em>v. <em>Duvall</em>, 740 F.&nbsp;3d 604, 610-611 (CADC 2013) (Kavanaugh, J., concurring in denial of rehearing en banc).  On very rare occasions, as in <em>Apodaca</em>, it can be difficult to discern which opinion's reasoning has precedential effect under <em>Marks.  </em>See also <em>Nichols</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 511 U.&nbsp;S. 738, 745-746 (1994) (analyzing <em>Baldasar</em> v. <em>Illinois</em>, 446 U.&nbsp;S. 222 (1980) (<em>per curiam</em>)).  But even when that happens, the <em>result</em> of the decision still constitutes a binding precedent for the federal and state courts, and for this Court, unless and until it is overruled by this Court.  As I read the Court's various opinions today, six Justices treat the result in <em>Apodaca </em>as a precedent for purposes of <em>stare decisis</em> analysis.  A different group of six Justices concludes that <em>Apodaca </em>should be and is overruled.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNopinion1.7" href="#FRopinion1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Oregon adopted the non-unanimous jury practice in 1934--one manifestation of the extensive 19th- and early 20th-century history of racist and anti-Semitic sentiment in that State.  See Kaplan &amp; Saack, Overturning <em>Apodaca v. Oregon</em> Should Be Easy: Nonunanimous Jury Verdicts in Criminal Cases Undermine the Credibility of Our Justice System, 95 Ore. L.&nbsp;Rev. 1, 3, 43-51 (2016); Mooney, Remembering 1857, 87 Ore. L.&nbsp;Rev. 731, 778, n.&nbsp;174 (2008).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNopinion1.8" href="#FRopinion1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;In <em>Allen </em>v. <em>Hardy</em>, 478 U.&nbsp;S. 255 (1986) (<em>per&nbsp;curiam</em>),<em> </em>this Court concluded--without briefing or oral argument--that <em>Batson </em>would not apply retroactively.  Under the well-settled <em>Teague </em>principles, there should be no doubt that today's decision likewise will not apply retroactively on collateral review.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNopinion1.9" href="#FRopinion1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Alito</small></span>'s characteristically incisive dissent rests largely on his view of the States' reliance interests.  My respectful disagreement with <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Alito</small></span> primarily boils down to our different assessments of those reliance interests--in particular, our different evaluations of how readily Louisiana and Oregon can adjust to an overruling of <em>Apodaca.</em></p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNopinion1.10" href="#FRopinion1.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;As noted above, I join the introduction and Parts I, II-A, III, and IV-B-1 of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Gorsuch</small></span>'s opinion for the Court.  The remainder of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Gorsuch</small></span>'s opinion does not command a majority.  That point is important with respect to Part IV-A, which only three Justices have joined.  It appears that six Justices of the Court treat the result in <em>Apodaca </em>as a precedent and therefore do not subscribe to the analysis in Part IV-A of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Gorsuch</small></span>'s opinion.</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3>
<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;I also note that, under my approach to <em>stare decisis</em>, there is no need to decide which reliance interests are important enough to save an incorrect precedent.  I doubt that this question is susceptible of principled resolution in this case, compare <em>ante,</em> at 22-26 (principal opinion), with <em>ante,</em> at 3 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor</small></span>, J., concurring); <em>ante,</em> at 15-17 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kavanaugh</small></span>, J., concurring); and <em>post</em>, at 19-26 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Alito</small></span>, J., dissenting), or in any other case for that matter, see, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>Kimble</em> v. <em>Marvel Entertainment, LLC</em>, 576 U.&nbsp;S. 446, 457-458 (2015); <em>Lawrence</em> v. <em>Texas</em>, 539 U.&nbsp;S. 558, 577 (2003); <em>Dickerson</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 530 U.&nbsp;S. 428, 443 (2000); <em>Planned Parenthood of Southeastern Pa.</em> v. <em>Casey</em>, 505 U.&nbsp;S. 833, 855-856 (1992).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Similarly, I express no view on how fundamental the right to unanimity is, what other attributes of a criminal jury are protected by the Privileges or Immunities Clause, what rights are protected in misdemeanor cases, or what rights are protected in civil trials.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNopinion1.8" href="#FRopinion1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The majority's defense of its reliance on the original reasons for the adoption of the Louisiana and Oregon rules is incoherent.  On the one hand, it asks: "[I]f the Sixth Amendment calls on judges to assess the functional benefits of jury rules, as the <em>Apodaca</em> plurality suggested, how can that analysis proceed to ignore the very functions those rules were adopted to serve?"  <em>Ante</em>, at 14, n. 44.  But three sentences later it answers its own question when it observes that "a jurisdiction adopting a nonunanimous jury rule for benign reasons today would still violate the Sixth Amendment."  <em>Ibid</em>.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Kavanaugh'</small></span>s<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small> </small></span>defense, see <em>ante</em>, at 13-15 (opinion concurring in part), is essentially the same.  After reiterating the history recounted by the majority, he eventually acknowledges that there are "neutral and legitimate" reasons for allowing non-unanimous verdicts and that Louisiana may have retained a version of its old rule for such  reasons.  He also agrees with the majority that a rule allowing nonunanimous verdicts would be unconstitutional no matter what the State's reasons.  So what is the relevance of the original motivations for the Louisiana and Oregon rules?  He offers no explanation.  He does opine that allowing such verdicts works to the disadvantage of African-American defendants, but the effect of various jury decision rules is a complex question that has been the subject of much social-science research, none of which the opinion even acknowledges.</p>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Contrary to the majority opinion, I am not arguing that the denial of certiorari is precedential.  See<em> ante</em>, at 19, n. 56.  My point, instead, is that the Court's pattern of denying review in cases presenting the question whether unanimity is required in state trials is evidence that this Court regarded <em>Apodaca</em> as a precedent.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 25" name="FNopinion1.25" href="#FRopinion1.25">25</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Five Justices in <em>Furman</em> found that the Eighth Amendment imposes an evolving standard of decency, 408 U.&nbsp;S., at 255-257 (Douglas, J., concurring); <em>id</em>., at 265-269 (Brennan, J., concurring); <em>id</em>., at 309-310 (Stewart, J., concurring); <em>id</em>., at  312-314 (White, J., concurring); <em>id.</em>, at 316, 322-333 (Marshall, J., concurring), and our subsequent cases have done the same.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 26" name="FNopinion1.26" href="#FRopinion1.26">26</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The majority also notes that the Judiciary Act of 1789 pegged the qualifications for service on federal juries to those used in the State in which a case was tried, <em>ante</em>, at 15, n. 47, but since all States barred women, see <em>Taylor</em>, 419 U.&nbsp;S., at 536, it is hard to see how the 1789 Act can provide a ground for distinguishing the common law's requirement of unanimity from its insistence that women were not fit to serve.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Jury practice at the time of the founding differed from current practice in other important respects. Jurors were not selected at random.  "[P]ublic officials called selectmen, supervisors, trustees, or 'sheriffs of the parish' exercised what Tocqueville called 'very extensive and very arbitrary' powers in summoning jurors."  Alschuler &amp; Deiss, A Brief History of the Criminal Jury in the United States, 61 U. Chi. L. Rev. 867, 879-880 (1994).  And "American trial judges . . . routinely summarized the evidence for jurors and often told jurors which witnesses they found most credible, and why."  Sklansky, Evidentiary Instructions and the Jury as Other, 65 Stan. L. Rev. 407, 454 (2013).  Any attempt to identify the aspects of late 18th-century practice that were incorporated into the Sixth Amendment should take the full picture into account and provide a principle for the distinction. </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 27" name="FNopinion1.27" href="#FRopinion1.27">27</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;As recently as 2010, prominent advocates urged us to hold that a provision of the Bill of Rights applies differently to the Federal Government and the States.  In <em>McDonald</em>, 561 U.&nbsp;S. 742, the city of Chicago and some of its <em>amici</em> argued that, despite our decision in <em>District of </em> <em>Columbia</em> v. <em>Heller</em>,<em> </em>554 U.&nbsp;S. 570 (2008), States and cities should be given leeway to regulate the possession of a firearm in the home for self-defense in accordance with the particular needs and desires of their citizens.  561 U.&nbsp;S., at 753.  Although this argument did not prevail, four Justices, some now in the majority, appeared to take that view.  See <em>id.</em>, at 927 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Breyer</small></span>, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>J</small></span>., joined by <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Ginsburg</small></span> and <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Sotomayor</small></span>, JJ., dissenting) (observing that "gun violence .&nbsp;.&nbsp;. varies as between rural communities and cities" and arguing that States and cities should be free to adopt rules that meet local needs and preferences); <em>id.</em>, at 866 (Stevens, J., dissenting) ("The rights protected against state infringement by the Fourteenth Amendment's Due Process Clause need not be identical in shape or scope to the rights protected against Federal Government infringement by the various provisions of the Bill of Rights").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 28" name="FNopinion1.28" href="#FRopinion1.28">28</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Ariz. Const., Art. 2, §30; Ark. Const., Amdt. 21, §1; Cal. Const., Art. I, §14; Colo. Rev. Stat. §16-5-205 (2019); Conn. Gen. Stat. §54-46 (2017); Haw. Const., Art. I, §10; Idaho Const., Art. I, §8; Ill. Comp. Stat., ch. 725, §5/111-2(a) (West 2018); Ind. Code §35-34-1-1(a) (2019); Iowa Ct. Rule 2.5 (2020); Kan. Stat. Ann. §22-3201 (2007); Md. Crim. Proc. Code Ann. §§4-102, 4-103 (2018); Mich. Comp. Laws §767.1 (1979); Mo. Const., Art. I, §17; Mont. Const., Art. II, §20(1); Neb. Rev. Stat. §29-1601 (2016); Nev. Const., Art. I, §8; N.&nbsp;M. Const., Art II, §14; N.&nbsp;D. Rule Crim. Proc. 7(a) (2018-2019); Okla. Const., Art II, §17; Ore. Const. (amended), Art. VII, §§5(3)-(5); Pa. Const., Art. I, §10 (providing that "[e]ach of the several courts of common pleas may, with the approval of the Supreme Court, provide for the initiation of criminal proceedings therein by  information"--a condition that has now been met in all counties); see also 42 Pa. Cons. Stat. §8931 (2015); S.&nbsp;D. Const., Art. VI, §10; Utah Const.,  Art. I, §13; Vt. Rule Crim. Proc. 7(a) (2018); Wash. Rev. Code §10.37.015 (2019); Wis. Stat. §967.05 (2015-2016); Wyo. Stat. Ann. §7-1-106(a) (2019).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 29" name="FNopinion1.29" href="#FRopinion1.29">29</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Ariz. Rev. Stat. Ann. §21-102 (2013); Conn. Gen. Stat. §54-82; Fla. Rule Crim. Proc. §3.270 (2019); Ind. Code §35-37-1-1(b)(2); Utah Code §78B-1-104 (2019).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 30" name="FNopinion1.30" href="#FRopinion1.30">30</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The majority arrives at a different figure based on the number of felony jury trials in Oregon in 2018, see <em>ante</em>, at 22-23, and n. 68, but it does not take 2019 into account.  And since we do not know how many cases remain on direct appeal, such calculations are unreliable.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 31" name="FNopinion1.31" href="#FRopinion1.31">31</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Under our case law, a State must give retroactive effect to any constitutional decision that is retroactive under the standard in <em>Teague </em>v.<em> Lane</em>, 489 U.&nbsp;S. 288 (1989), but it may adopt a broader retroactivity rule.  <em>Montgomery</em> v. <em>Louisiana</em>, 577 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2016); <em>Danforth</em> v. <em>Minnesota</em>, 552 U.&nbsp;S. 264, 275 (2008).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 32" name="FNopinion1.32" href="#FRopinion1.32">32</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Studies show that when a supermajority votes for a verdict near the beginning of deliberations, a unanimous verdict is usually reached.  See generally Devine, Clayton, Dunford, Seying, &amp; Price, Jury Decision Making: 45 Years of Empirical Research on Deliberating Groups, 7 Psychology Pub. Pol'y &amp; L. 622, 690-707 (2001).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 33" name="FNopinion1.33" href="#FRopinion1.33">33</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The reliance in this case also far exceeds that in <em>Arizona </em>v.<em> Gant</em>, 556 U.&nbsp;S. 332 (2009), where the Court effectively overruled a decision, <em>New York </em>v.<em> Belton</em>, 453 U.&nbsp;S. 454 (1981), that allowed a police officer to search the entire passenger compartment of a car if the officer had probable cause to arrest the driver or a passenger.  556 U.&nbsp;S., at 335.  Police departments had trained officers in reliance on the <em>Belton</em> rule, see <em>Gant</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 358-360 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Alito, J.,</small></span> dissenting), but the burden of retraining cannot compare with conducting a large number of retrials and potentially releasing defendants who cannot be retried due to post-trial events.</p>
 
        <p></p>
        <p></p>
    </div>

</div>

    <section class="latl-section section"><div class="latl-section__body">
    <div class="latl-section__text-content">
        <h2 class="latl-section__title">Learn About the Law</h2>
        <p class="latl-section__subtitle">Get help with your legal needs</p>
        <div class="latl-section__paragraph"><p>FindLaw’s Learn About the Law features thousands of informational articles to help you understand your options. _satellite.ads = {};

_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory = function(nameString, href) {
  // TODO remove this once the correct PA is being used for the lid
  if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') && _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa ) {
    nameString = nameString.replace(/pa:[0-9]+/, "pa:"+_satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa);  
  }
  _satellite.logger.debug('_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory nameString: '+nameString);

  // sample name: "&lid=clk:fs/prod:dts/cid:5088127/msa:259/pa:1313/sub:3107462/pos:1"
  var s = nameString;
  s = s.substr(5);
  var lid = s;
  s = s.replaceAll(':','":"');
  s = s.replaceAll('/','","');
  s = '{"'+s+'"}'
  var adInfo = JSON.parse(s);
  adInfo.lid = lid;
  adInfo.paid = (typeof adInfo.sub != 'undefined');
  adInfo.adId = (adInfo.paid ? adInfo.sub : adInfo.cid);
  var map = {
    'ph':'phone',
    'fs':'website',
    'pv':'profile',
    'cu':'contact',
    'vr':'review-read',
    'wr':'review-write',
    'sl':'super-lawyers'
  };  
  adInfo.type = map[adInfo.clk];   
  adInfo.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id')+'-'+adInfo.adId+'-'+(new Date().getTime());
  
  if( href && adInfo.type == 'phone' ) {
    adInfo.detail = href.replace(/[^\d]/g,'');
    if( adInfo.detail.length > 10 ) {
      adInfo.detail = adInfo.detail.substr(adInfo.detail.length-10);
    }
  }  
  
  return adInfo;
}

_satellite.ads.trackAdEngagement = function(adInfo) {
  if( typeof adInfo.adId == 'undefined' ) {
    return false;
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads = {};
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements = [];
  }
  for( var i=0; i < FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.length; i++ ) {
    if( FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].type == adInfo.type && FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].adId == adInfo.adId ) {
      return true;
    }
  }

  FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.push(adInfo);
  return false;
}

_satellite.ads.sendConversionFindLaw = function() {
  var subId = _satellite.getVar('current_ad_eng').sub;
  var cookieName = 'fl_ad_'+subId;
  var cookieValue = 'true';
  var c = _satellite.cookie.get(cookieName);
  // we use a cookie to ensure multiple beacons are not sent for the same ad
  if( typeof c == 'undefined' ) {
    _satellite.setFlCookie(cookieName, cookieValue, 1);

    var data = {};
    var clickUrlElement = document.getElementById(_satellite.getVar('ad_eng_sub'));
    if (clickUrlElement !== null) {
      data.clickUrl = clickUrlElement.innerText;
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') ) {
      data.adDecisionContext = _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_transaction_id') ) {
      data.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_transaction_id');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_sub') ) {
      data.subId = _satellite.getVar('ad_eng_sub');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id') ) {
      data.flUserId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id');
    }
    if( _satellite.getVar('fl_tag_track') == 'false' ) {
      data.internalFlag = 'true'; // mimick as an internal request
    }

    let url = new URL(window.location.href);
    let domain = 'attorneys.findlaw'+(_satellite.getVar('cms_env') == 'prod' ? '' : '-stage')+'.com';
    let attorneysUrl = new URL('https://'+domain+'/flstats/v1/stats');
    let searchParams = new URLSearchParams(window.location.search);

    if (searchParams.has('ads-server-env')) {
      attorneysUrl.searchParams.set('ads-server-env', url.searchParams.get('ads-server-env'));
    }
    navigator.sendBeacon(attorneysUrl.href, JSON.stringify(data));
  }
}

};

function setTrackerCookie(name, value)
{
  var hostParts = location.host.split('.').reverse();
  var cookieDomain = '.' + hostParts[1] + '.' + hostParts[0];
  document.cookie = name + '=' + value + ';domain=' + cookieDomain + ';path=/';
}
